*The Last Mind* is a five-volume work documenting the contemporary global institutional architecture that produces the wars, the inequality, the regulatory capture, and the consensus-knowledge capture of the contemporary world. The five volumes are:

- **Volume One: *Behind the Mask: The Devil The Demons Call God*** — the contemporary financial system as the operational extension of the four-century debt-money architecture (published 13 May 2026).
- **Volume Two: *Behind the Performance: The Demon They Call the State*** — the contemporary global political class as the operational extension of the same mechanism (published 18 May 2026; revised and expanded 21 May 2026).
- **Volume Three: *Behind the Blessed: The Demon They Call the Church*** — the contemporary religious-institutional architecture (published 21 May 2026).
- **Volume Four: *Behind the Knowing: The Demon They Call Consensus*** — the contemporary consensus-knowledge apparatus (published 21 May 2026).
- **Volume Five: *Behind the Synthesis: The Devil, The Demons, and The Masks*** — the foundational entry volume drawing the four masks together into one explanatory framework (this volume).

This volume is the foundation under the other four. The reader holding *Behind the Synthesis* in hand does not need to have read the other four to follow what is written here. The other four go deeper. This one explains what they document, in language that asks nothing of the reader except attention.

## How to read this book

If you are a new reader who has never opened any volume of *The Last Mind* before, you can begin here. This book introduces every term used across the series in plain language. You will finish it with the foundation you need to read the other four volumes if you want to go deeper. You will also finish it with the picture in full at the level of what is happening and why — even if you read no further.

If you have read one or more of the prior volumes, this book consolidates what you have read against the other layers you may not have. The financial reader sees how the financial mask connects to the religious mask. The political reader sees how the political mask connects to the epistemic mask. The reader who has read all four sees them drawn together as one system.

If you are an academic, a journalist, or a policy researcher engaging the series for the first time, Chapter 1 carries the theoretical formulation at the register your work requires. You can extract it as a standalone framework and follow the citations into the documentary substantiation in Parts IV and V.

The series is published free of charge. Every volume of *The Last Mind* is free to read. This volume will be free. You do not have to take any part of what follows on faith. The proofs are on the page. The page is free. The proofs can be checked. They should be.

## A note on language

Every technical term in this book is explained the first time it appears, in plain words, in the same sentence or the very next one. Where a term recurs many pages later, the meaning is anchored again in the surrounding prose so a reader who has lost the thread does not have to flip back. Every named person is introduced from scratch — who they are, when they lived, what they did, why they appear here, where in the series they are dossiered at length. Every named institution is treated the same way.

A reader who finds a term unclear at any point can also consult the Glossary at the back, which gathers every term used across all five volumes in one place.

The standard is that any reader of any age, with no background in finance, politics, theology, or science, can finish this book and have the foundation to read the rest of the series. If at any point a sentence asks more of you than that, that is a failure of the writing — the writing should be rewritten, not the reader's understanding stretched to meet the writing where it failed.

## What this volume is

This volume is not a theory. It is a documented account.

The publishing convention calls a book of this kind a "unifying theory" because that is the label readers and reviewers recognise. Use that label if it helps. The work itself does not theorise. It synthesises a documented record into one coherent account and explains the record in language any reader can follow.

The record is built on four prior volumes of documented evidence: financial filings, court rulings, government documents, primary institutional reports, peer-reviewed papers, contemporary investigative reporting, and the public statements of the figures and institutions the volumes dossier. The record is published. The record is free to read. The record can be checked.

This volume names what the record shows when read together.

The structure of the volume is three terms taken from old religious vocabulary because the vocabulary names what is being described better than the polite contemporary alternatives do. The three terms are:

- **The Devil** — the singular root cause beneath the architecture. A *root cause* is the underlying thing that produces a pattern of outcomes. The Devil names what the record shows is producing the extraction of wealth, time, attention, and consent that the four prior volumes describe.
- **The Demons** — the operational nodes through which the Devil works. An *operational node* is a person or institution through which a larger pattern is actually carried out in practice. The Demons are the named figures and the named institutions whose activities carry the extraction into the world.
- **The Masks** — the public-facing surfaces under which the operation runs. A *mask*, in the sense the volume uses, is a system that presents itself to the public as one thing while functioning operationally as something else. The volume names four such masks — finance, politics, religion, and knowledge — and a fifth mask that hides the integration of the other four.

The volume walks through each in turn. Before that, the volume walks through the vocabulary the record uses. That walkthrough is Part 0 of this book.

# Prologue: What Has Been Documented

You are reading the foundational volume of a five-volume work that documents how, over four hundred years, a small group of people working through banks, governments, religious institutions, and the bodies that produce what counts as knowledge has been transferring wealth from a much larger group of people to themselves. The four prior volumes have already laid the evidence out, layer by layer. This volume explains what the layers show when read together, and writes the explanation in plain language so that any reader — including a reader who has never opened any of the other four volumes — can follow it.

If that opening claim sounds large to you, the rest of the volume walks through why the documented evidence supports it. You do not have to take the claim on faith. The record is published. The record is free to read. You do not need a degree or specialist background to check it. You need attention and the foundational vocabulary that the chapters of Part 0 will give you.

This Prologue states what the four prior volumes have established, names the structure of the account the rest of the volume will walk through, and acknowledges the conflict of interest under which this volume is written. It is short on purpose. The detail comes in the chapters that follow.

## What the four prior volumes have established

**Volume One — *Behind the Mask: The Devil The Demons Call God*** documents the contemporary financial system. Its central finding is that the way money is created and managed in the contemporary world rests on a mechanism that began in early-seventeenth-century Amsterdam and late-seventeenth-century London. The mechanism produces wealth for a small group by taking it from a larger group. The mechanism cannot stop producing this transfer because the way it is designed requires the transfer to continue. Volume One dossiers the contemporary figures and institutions through which the mechanism operates at scale. The institutional layer includes the central banks (one per country or currency-area, the institutions that issue official money) and the largest commercial banks (the ordinary banks where people open accounts). It also covers the principal asset-management firms (companies like BlackRock that manage other people's money in pooled investment vehicles) and the sovereign wealth funds (the giant pools of money held and invested by states). Alongside these institutions, Volume One dossiers the contemporary technology-and-capital figures whose fortunes the mechanism has produced. Each of these terms is explained from first principles in Chapter 0.1 of this volume.

**Volume Two — *Behind the Performance: The Demon They Call the State*** documents the contemporary global political class. Its central finding is that governments across the world — democracies and autocracies alike — do not function as the sovereign decision-making bodies they present themselves as. They function as the enforcement arm of the financial mechanism Volume One named. Volume Two dossiers fifty principal contemporary political figures across the world, from the United States, the United Kingdom, and the European Union to China, Russia, Saudi Arabia, Brazil, India, South Africa, and Nigeria, and shows the documented pattern in each case. The political-class vocabulary is explained from first principles in Chapter 0.2 of this volume.

**Volume Three — *Behind the Blessed: The Demon They Call the Church*** documents the contemporary religious-institutional architecture. Its central finding is that the institutional churches across every major tradition operate as moral cover for the same financial mechanism. The traditions covered include the Catholic hierarchy in Rome, the Protestant denominations, the Russian Orthodox Church, the Saudi-funded Sunni Islamic institutional apparatus, the Iranian Shia institutional apparatus, the captured Hindu nationalist positioning in contemporary India, and the captured Buddhist institutional hierarchies in Southeast and East Asia. The volume distinguishes between religious traditions (the spiritual practices and lived faith of communities) and religious institutions (the captured hierarchies that claim to speak for those traditions). The distinction is explained from first principles in Chapter 0.3 of this volume.

**Volume Four — *Behind the Knowing: The Demon They Call Consensus*** documents the contemporary architecture that produces what counts as authoritative knowledge. The volume covers the captured press, the captured academic publishing industry, the captured peer-review apparatus, the captured public-health institutions, and the captured climate-policy bodies. It also covers the captured artificial-intelligence laboratories, the captured think tanks (research institutions that influence policy), the captured fact-checking organisations, and the captured search engines. Its central finding is that the trust people place in knowledge — when a doctor says a medicine is safe, when a scientist says a finding is true, when a journalist says a story is accurate — has been routed through institutions captured by the same financial mechanism. The vocabulary of consensus and capture is explained from first principles in Chapter 0.4 of this volume.

The four volumes were published across May 2026. Together they run to roughly one million words of documented investigative reporting. They are free to read. Their citations are on the page. The contested-record items are named where contested rather than adjudicated.

## What this volume names

The four volumes, read together, document one operation rather than four. The operation operates through four masks because no single mask could carry the weight of what the operation does. A financial system that openly admitted what it was doing would not survive public consent. A political class that openly admitted what it was doing would not survive an election. A religious institution that openly admitted what it was doing would not survive the loss of faith. A knowledge apparatus that openly admitted what it was doing would not survive the loss of credibility. The four masks make the operation survivable by distributing the appearance of legitimacy across four sectors that present themselves as independent.

This volume names the structure of the operation in three parts:

**The Devil** is the singular root cause. The thing that drives the whole operation. The four-century debt-money mechanism, which Chapter 7 of Volume One documents at length and Chapter 2 of this volume restates at the synthesis register, is the candidate the record supports. The mechanism requires continuous extraction because of how it is structured. The continuous extraction requires the four masks because no single mask could carry the population's consent to continuous extraction across four hundred years.

**The Demons** are the operational nodes. The named figures whose activities run the masks. The named institutions through which the figures operate. The senior demons among the figures are people whose names appear repeatedly across the four prior volumes. George Soros, the Hungarian-American financier, is dossiered in Volume One Chapter 14, Volume Two Chapter 9, and Volume Four Chapter 13. Bill Gates, the Microsoft founder, is dossiered in Volume One Chapter 13, Volume Three Chapter 18, and Volume Four Chapter 11. Dustin Moskovitz, the Facebook co-founder, is dossiered in Volume Four Chapter 12 alongside Holden Karnofsky and the effective-altruism funding architecture. Mohammed bin Salman, the Saudi Crown Prince, is dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 24 and Volume Three Chapter 22. Vladimir Putin is dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 19 and Volume Three Chapter 11. Other senior figures are dossiered across all four prior volumes. The institutional demons are the captured bodies through which the figures operate. BlackRock is the world's largest asset manager, holding roughly $11 trillion in assets under management as of 2025; it is dossiered in Volume One Chapter 10. The World Economic Forum is the Davos-based annual meeting and policy-coordination body, dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 6. The captured central banks include the Federal Reserve, the European Central Bank, the Bank of England, the People's Bank of China, and the Bank of Japan, dossiered across Volume One Chapters 5 and 6. The captured fact-checking organisations include the network around the Poynter Institute's International Fact-Checking Network, dossiered in Volume Four Chapter 7. The captured AI laboratories include OpenAI, Anthropic, and Google DeepMind, dossiered in Volume Four Chapter 9. Each figure and each institution is named again from scratch in the chapter of this volume where they recur, so the reader who is encountering them here for the first time has the introduction they need.

**The Masks** are the four operational layers themselves, plus the fifth mask that hides their integration:
- The **market mask** — the financial system documented in Volume One
- The **state mask** — the political-class architecture documented in Volume Two
- The **church mask** — the religious-institutional architecture documented in Volume Three
- The **consensus mask** — the captured-knowledge apparatus documented in Volume Four
- The **unity mask** — the contemporary architectures of global cooperation, governance, security, public health, climate response, AI safety, and global civil society. These present the four operational masks as integrated for good reasons. The record shows they are integrated to extract.

## The compact statement

The record, taken together, supports the following claim in plain language:

> For four hundred years, the way money is created and managed has produced wealth for a small group of people by taking it from a larger group of people. Over time, that small group has come to control not just the banks, but also the governments that make the laws, the churches that shape morality, and the people who decide what counts as knowledge. These four control points work together as one system. They look like four separate things — markets, states, churches, science — but they share the same operators, the same money flows, the same connections. If they were four separate things, you should be able to find places where they operate independently. The record shows you cannot.

The same claim stated in the language of academic, policy, and journalism work — the language used to engage with peer-reviewers, think-tank readers, and editorial boards:

> A four-century debt-money mechanism originating in the early-modern European banking-and-sovereign-debt architecture has, by continuous historical operation, integrated four operational layers — finance, political administration, religious institution, and consensus knowledge — into a single coordinated extraction system. Each operational layer functions as a mask: a surface that presents the integrated system as four independent sectors. The integration is documented at the level of named operators, financial flows, cross-sector vehicles, and revolving-door pathways. The claim is falsified if any one of the four documented layers can be shown to operate independent of the others at the level of senior operators, financial flows, and cross-sector coordination over the documented period 1602-2026.

Both statements name the same account. The first version is the version you tell a friend. The second is the version you submit to a journal. The substance is identical. Chapter 1 of this volume returns to both versions and walks through what each term in the formal version means, what would falsify the claim, and why both versions describe a documented account rather than a theoretical speculation.

The chapter that follows this Prologue — Chapter 0.1 — begins Part 0. Part 0 walks through the foundational vocabulary the rest of the volume uses. Chapter 0.1 covers money, debt, and banks. Chapter 0.2 covers states, political classes, and capture. Chapter 0.3 covers institutions, authority, and the distinction between religious traditions and religious institutions. Part I — Chapters 2 through 5 — walks through the Devil. The four-century debt-money mechanism, restated at the synthesis register, with worked examples drawn from the record.

Part II — Chapters 6 through 11 — walks through the Demons. The named figures and institutions, presented at the integrated level that the four prior volumes individually established. The chapter on cross-mask operators (Chapter 10) carries worked examples that show how a single figure can operate across two or more masks at once.

The formal statement of the account, the conditions under which it could be falsified, the operational definitions of every term in it, and the methodological grounds for treating it as a documented account rather than a polemic.

Part VII closes the volume with what the series title names and what is left.

The back matter carries the per-chapter source citations, the Author's Note, the Acknowledgements and Omissions, the Glossary that defines every term used across all five volumes, the Index of Names that lists every figure dossiered across the series, and the Credits.

## What this volume does not do

This volume does not predict the future. The record concerns what has happened and what is happening. The volume does not extrapolate forward beyond what the record supports.

This volume does not theorise beyond the account. Where the record stops, the volume stops.

This volume does not adjudicate scientific or empirical disputes the four prior volumes dossier. Where the record is contested at the documentary level, the contestation is named rather than resolved.

This volume does not soften the record to make it easier to read. The findings of the four prior volumes are the findings of the four prior volumes. The synthesis presented here is the synthesis the record supports. The reader who finds the synthesis hard to accept is invited to check the record. The record is published. The record is free.

# Chapter 0.1: What Money Is, What Debt Is, What a Bank Is

This is the first chapter of Part 0 of *Behind the Synthesis*. Part 0 walks through the foundational vocabulary the rest of the volume uses. This chapter covers money, debt, banks, and the four-hundred-year architecture in which the three sit together. By the end of the chapter the reader will have the vocabulary to follow every later chapter of this volume and every chapter of Volume One of the series.

The chapter is long because it carries the foundational weight. Every later chapter rests on what is established here. If a paragraph asks more of the reader than attention, the paragraph is rewritten until it does not. Where a term is introduced for the first time, it is explained in the same sentence. Where the term recurs many pages later, the meaning is anchored again in the surrounding prose. The reader who is encountering the vocabulary for the first time should be able to follow without flipping back.

## I. What money actually is

Take a banknote out of your pocket. A ten-pound note, a twenty-dollar bill, a hundred-rupee note — whichever currency you carry. Look at it. The paper is worth, as paper, perhaps a fraction of a penny. The ink is worth less. The note itself, as a physical object, is worth almost nothing. And yet you can take that note to a shop and exchange it for food, or for clothes, or for a bus ticket, or for an hour of someone else's labour.

Why?

The answer most people would give is something like: because the government says so, or because the note is backed by gold, or because the bank guarantees it. None of these answers is quite right. The actual answer is this: money is a social-and-legal agreement that the note represents a claim on goods, services, and labour. The agreement is enforced by law and by custom. The agreement holds because enough people accept it. If the agreement collapsed — if everyone in your country woke up tomorrow and refused to accept the currency — the notes in your pocket would become what they are physically: paper.

This is not a metaphor. This is what money is. It is not a thing. It is a relationship — a relationship between you, the people who accept the note from you, the government that issued the note, and the institutions that stand behind the note. When the relationship works, money works. When the relationship fails, money fails. The relationship has failed many times in modern history. It failed in the German Weimar Republic in 1923, when a wheelbarrow full of marks would not buy a loaf of bread. It failed in Hungary in 1946, in what remains the worst documented hyperinflation in recorded history, when prices doubled every fifteen hours. It failed in Zimbabwe in the late 2000s, when the country's central bank issued a hundred-trillion-dollar note that could not pay a bus fare. It has failed periodically in Argentina across most of the twentieth century and into the twenty-first, and in Venezuela through the 2010s. Each time the social agreement collapsed, the currency collapsed with it.

There is a technical word for this kind of money — money whose value comes from social-and-legal agreement rather than from any physical commodity behind it. The word is **fiat money**, from the Latin *fiat*, meaning "let it be done." Fiat money is money because the state has declared it money and enough people accept the declaration. Every banknote in your wallet right now is fiat money. The pound, the dollar, the euro, the yen, the renminbi, the rupee — every contemporary national currency is fiat money. None is backed by gold. None has been backed by gold since 15 August 1971, when President Richard Nixon ended the **convertibility** (the ability to exchange one form of money for another at a stated rate) of the United States dollar into gold. The dollar had been the reserve currency that other major currencies pegged themselves to under the post-war international financial arrangement called the **Bretton Woods system** (after the 1944 conference at Bretton Woods, New Hampshire, where it was agreed). When Nixon ended the dollar's convertibility into gold, the rest of the world's currencies came off gold with it. That day — 15 August 1971 — is one of the most important dates in the modern history of money. Volume One Chapter 6 of *The Last Mind* series dossiers the events of August 1971 and the architecture that emerged from them at length.

So when you hold a banknote, you are holding the physical token of a social-and-legal agreement. The agreement says you can spend it. The agreement does not say what backs it, because nothing backs it in the physical-commodity sense. What backs it is the willingness of millions of other people to also accept the agreement. This is the first thing to understand about modern money.

## II. Where the money actually comes from

The question seems simple. Most people have a vague answer: the government prints it. This is the textbook answer most schools teach. It is also wrong.

The actual answer is more interesting and more important. There are two sources of the money you use, and the second source produces roughly ninety-seven percent of it.

**The first source is the central bank.** A **central bank** is an institution, usually one per country or currency-area, authorised by the government to issue the country's official money. The central bank of the United States is the **Federal Reserve System** (commonly called "the Fed"), founded on 23 December 1913 under the Federal Reserve Act signed by President Woodrow Wilson. The central bank of the United Kingdom is the **Bank of England**, founded in 1694, which Section V of this chapter will return to in detail. The central bank of the **Eurozone** — the group of European Union member countries that share the euro as their currency — is the **European Central Bank**, founded on 1 June 1998 and headquartered in Frankfurt, Germany. The central bank of Japan is the **Bank of Japan**, founded on 27 June 1882 in Tokyo. The central bank of China is the **People's Bank of China**, formally restructured as China's central bank in 1995. Volume One Chapter 5 of the series dossiers the Federal Reserve at the operational level; Volume One Chapter 6 covers the contemporary global central-banking coordination architecture across the Federal Reserve, the European Central Bank, the Bank of England, the Bank of Japan, and the People's Bank of China.

The central bank issues physical money — the notes printed and the coins minted under government authority. The central bank also issues a different kind of money, which exists only as numbers in electronic accounts. These accounts are held by commercial banks (the kind of banks where ordinary people open current and savings accounts) at the central bank itself. The money in these accounts is called **central bank reserves**. Together, the physical notes and coins in circulation plus the central bank reserves held at the central bank are what economists call the **monetary base**, sometimes also called **base money** or **narrow money**. In the United Kingdom, the official statistical name for narrow money is **M0**. According to the Bank of England's published statistics, the United Kingdom's M0 in February 2026 stood at roughly £104 billion.

So far, so straightforward: the central bank issues the money. This is what the textbook says. The textbook is also where the textbook stops.

**The second source — and this is the one that produces almost all the money in circulation — is the ordinary commercial bank.** A **commercial bank** is a profit-making private corporation licensed by the government to take in deposits, make loans, process payments, and provide other financial services. The five largest commercial banks in the world by total assets, as of 2025, are the Industrial and Commercial Bank of China (ICBC), the Agricultural Bank of China, the China Construction Bank, the Bank of China, and JPMorgan Chase, in that order. The top four are all Chinese state-owned banks. JPMorgan Chase is the largest non-Chinese bank in the world. ICBC holds total assets in the range of $5.7 to $6.6 trillion depending on the reporting period (sources include S&P Global Market Intelligence and CompaniesMarketCap, April 2026). JPMorgan Chase held $4.425 trillion in total assets as of 31 December 2025 according to S&P Global Market Intelligence. The largest European commercial bank, HSBC Holdings (headquartered in London), held $2.99 trillion as of mid-2025. Together, the world's fifty largest banks hold roughly $101.6 trillion in assets — a figure approaching the world's total $111 trillion government debt load in 2025 (Visual Capitalist, *Ranked: The World's 50 Largest Banks by Assets*, April 2026). Volume One Chapter 12 of the series dossiers JPMorgan Chase at the operational level; Volume One Chapter 17 dossiers the Chinese state-owned banking sector; Volume One Chapters 8 through 11 dossier the broader global commercial-banking architecture.

The crucial fact about commercial banks — the fact most people are not taught, the fact that changes how you read the rest of *The Last Mind* — is this. When a commercial bank makes a loan, the bank does not lend out money that has been deposited with it by other customers. The bank creates new money at the moment the loan is made.

This claim sounds wrong if you have been taught the textbook story. So the claim needs to be walked through carefully.

The textbook story says: depositors put money into the bank. The bank holds some of it in reserve, then lends most of it out to borrowers. Depositors are paid a low rate of interest. Borrowers are charged a higher rate. The institution keeps the difference. The bank, in this story, is a kind of warehouse for money. It lends out what others have stored.

That is not what banks do. That is not how the system works.

What banks actually do, when they make a loan, is this. The borrower walks in (or applies online) and asks for a loan — say, £10,000. The bank assesses the borrower's **creditworthiness** (the bank's judgement of whether the borrower is likely to repay). If the loan is approved, the bank does two things at the same time on its accounting books. On one side of the books, the bank records that the borrower now owes the bank £10,000 — this is a new **asset** (something the bank owns, in this case the borrower's promise to repay). On the other side of the books, the bank credits the borrower's deposit account with £10,000 — this is a new **liability** (something the bank owes, in this case the £10,000 the borrower can now spend). The £10,000 in the borrower's account did not come from anyone else's deposits. It did not come from the central bank. It did not come from anywhere. It was typed into the account at the moment the loan was approved. The lender's books balance because the asset (£10,000 the borrower owes the bank) matches the liability (£10,000 the bank owes the borrower in the borrower's account). The bank has created new money. The borrower can now spend the £10,000. When the borrower spends it, it becomes new money in other people's accounts.

When the borrower eventually repays the loan, the process runs in reverse. The borrower transfers £10,000 from their account back to the bank. The bank's asset (the loan) is cancelled. The bank's liability (the deposit) is cancelled. The £10,000 has been destroyed. It no longer exists.

If you find this claim hard to believe, you do not have to take it on faith from this chapter. The claim is the official position of the Bank of England — the central bank of the United Kingdom, the same institution William Paterson and his co-founders established in 1694. In March 2014, three economists from the Bank's Monetary Analysis Directorate (the Bank of England's department for studying how money works) published a paper called *Money Creation in the Modern Economy* in the Bank's Quarterly Bulletin. The three authors were Michael McLeay, Amar Radia, and Ryland Thomas. Their paper is published on the Bank of England's own website and remains freely available there. The paper's central conclusion is in its opening summary, where the authors state that "the majority of money in the modern economy is created by commercial banks making loans." The paper goes on to state that banks "do not act simply as intermediaries, lending out deposits that savers place with them, and nor do they 'multiply up' central bank money to create new loans and deposits." The textbook story is wrong. The central bank of the United Kingdom has said so in print since 2014. The paper is dossiered at the operational level in Volume One Chapter 5 of the series.

This is the official admission against interest — an admission, by an institution, of something that does not serve the institution's interests to admit. The institution that benefits most from people believing the textbook story has, in its own quarterly bulletin, said the textbook story is wrong. The actual story is the one in the paragraphs above.

A separate empirical study by the German-British economist **Richard Werner** (Professor of International Banking, then at the University of Southampton), published in the same year in the journal *International Review of Financial Analysis*, confirmed the Bank of England's account through a controlled experiment. Werner arranged for a single loan to be processed by a small German commercial lender and traced the bank's accounting entries in real time, demonstrating that the loan was funded by money the bank created at the moment of lending, not by money intermediated from other depositors. Werner's study is the experimental verification of what the Bank of England paper describes. The Bank of England paper and Werner's empirical study are both in the Sources file for this chapter.

The reason this matters — and it matters enough that the whole of *The Last Mind* rests on it — is that it changes what a commercial banking institution actually is. A commercial banking institution is not a warehouse for other people's money. A commercial bank is an institution licensed by the government to create money. Every time a bank makes a loan, new money enters the economy. Every time a loan is repaid, money leaves the economy. The total quantity of money in circulation in any country at any time is set by the difference between new loans being created and old loans being repaid, plus the central-banking sector's much smaller contribution to the stock of physical notes and reserves.

The proportions can be checked in the published statistics of any major central bank. In the United Kingdom, the Bank of England publishes figures for M0 (the narrow money: physical notes plus central bank reserves) and for **M4** (the broad measure of money, which includes commercial-bank deposits — that is, the money in ordinary people's bank accounts). At the end of 2025, the United Kingdom's M0 stood at roughly £104 billion. The United Kingdom's M4 stood at roughly £3.21 trillion. M4 was roughly thirty times M0. The portion that is narrow money — the central bank's notes and reserves — was roughly three percent of the total. The portion that is commercial-bank deposits was roughly ninety-seven percent. (These figures come from the Bank of England's published statistical series for M0 and M4, accessed at the Bank's website and aggregated by Trading Economics, January–February 2026.)

So when someone says "the government prints the money," they are roughly speaking about three percent of what circulates as money. The other ninety-seven percent is created by ordinary commercial banks every time they make a loan. The system rests on debt because the system creates money as debt. Money and debt, in the contemporary economy, are the two sides of the same accounting entry — the asset and the liability that come into existence together when a loan is made, and that go out of existence together when the loan is repaid.

This is the foundational fact about contemporary money. The next section walks through what debt itself actually is.

## III. What debt actually is

Debt is older than money. The conventional textbook story descends from the Scottish economist **Adam Smith** (1723-1790) in his 1776 book *An Inquiry into the Nature and Causes of the Wealth of Nations*, generally known as *The Wealth of Nations*. Smith's account says that early human communities **bartered** (traded goods directly: my chicken for your bushel of wheat), found barter inefficient because of what economists call the **coincidence-of-wants problem** (I have a chicken to trade but want cloth; the cloth-maker wants shoes, not chicken; so no trade happens), and invented money to solve the problem. Once money existed, the story goes, credit and debt followed as a more sophisticated extension of money. The order in Smith's story is: barter → money → debt.

The anthropological and primary-document record overturns this story. The principal contemporary scholarly work documenting the overturn is *Debt: The First 5,000 Years* (Melville House, 2011) by the late American-born anthropologist **David Graeber** (1961-2020). Graeber, who was Professor of Anthropology at the London School of Economics at the time of his death, worked from the cuneiform tablets at the Sumerian temple complexes of the third millennium BCE — the earliest extensive written records of any human society. What Graeber documents, drawing on these primary sources alongside the work of earlier anthropologists, is that the historical and ethnographic record shows almost no evidence of the barter society Smith described. Pure barter as a primary organising principle of economic life appears in no documented human community at scale.

What the record does show, in Mesopotamia from the third millennium BCE onward and parallel records from Egypt, India, and China, is **credit and debt** — relationships in which one party would owe something to another, recorded in a way both parties accepted, and settled later. A farmer brought grain to the Sumerian temple complex. The temple recorded the grain as a credit to the farmer. The farmer later took something else of equivalent value — a tool, a piece of cloth, an animal — and the temple debited the credit. The credit was a debt the temple owed the farmer until it was settled. Coined money — the kind of money Smith took as starting point — did not appear in the documented historical record until the seventh century BCE, in Lydia in what is now western Turkey. Coined money appeared roughly two thousand years after the first documented debt relationships. The actual order, in the record, is debt first, then money, with barter scarcely appearing as a primary economic principle in any documented community.

This is the credit theory of money. The position was set out by the British diplomat and economist **Alfred Mitchell-Innes** in two 1913-1914 papers in the *Banking Law Journal*, and developed across the twentieth century by economists including **Geoffrey Ingham** (Cambridge) in *The Nature of Money* (Polity Press, 2004). The position holds that money is at root credit, and credit is at root a social relationship in which one party owes another. The commodity theory of money — that money is a physical commodity like gold that became universal as a medium of exchange — is the textbook position that descends from Smith. The documentary record supports the credit theory. The textbook position remains the standard taught in most introductory economics courses. Volume One Chapter 4 of the series dossiers the institutional reasons the textbook position has persisted in formal economics teaching despite the documentary evidence against it.

What debt actually is, then, at the foundational level, is a relationship in which one party owes something to another, recorded in a way both parties accept. The "something" can be goods, services, labour, money, or any other thing of value. The relationship can be between two individuals, between an individual and an institution, or between two institutions. The recording can be on cuneiform tablets, on parchment, on paper, in account books, or on the electronic ledgers of contemporary banks. The form changes. The relationship is the same.

Modern debt — the kind you have on a credit card, on a mortgage, on a student loan — is the same relationship in a more elaborate form. You owe a bank a specific sum of money, repayable over a specific period, at a specific rate of **interest** (a payment for the use of the money over time, expressed as a percentage of the amount borrowed per year).

Interest is the crucial addition. Many of the earliest documented debt relationships in Mesopotamia did not involve interest. Where interest existed, it was often capped by law, and there were periodic **debt cancellations** — moments at which all debts were forgiven, freeing debtors from their obligations. The Mesopotamian rulers periodically declared "clean slate" amnesties: in Sumerian *amargi* (literally "return to mother"), in Akkadian *andurarum*. The biblical **Jubilee**, described in the Book of Leviticus chapter 25 of the Hebrew Bible, is a version of the same institution: every fiftieth year, debts were cancelled and bonded labourers freed. The American-born economist **Michael Hudson** (Distinguished Research Professor at the University of Missouri-Kansas City), in his 2018 book *...and forgive them their debts: Lending, Foreclosure and Redemption from Bronze Age Finance to the Jubilee Year* (ISLET Verlag), documents the Mesopotamian and biblical debt-cancellation traditions from the cuneiform record. Volume One Chapter 4 dossiers the transition from the debt-cancellation tradition to the contemporary no-cancellation system at the institutional level.

The contemporary debt system does not have debt cancellations as a routine feature. The contemporary system treats debts as obligations that must be paid in full, regardless of the consequences for the debtor. The shift from the debt-cancellation tradition to the contemporary arrangement is part of the four-century mechanism this series documents.

The contemporary debt system also rests on **compound interest**. Compound interest means that interest accrues on the unpaid interest as well as on the original debt. If you owe £100 at 10% compound annual interest and pay nothing for ten years, you owe £259.37. After twenty years you owe £672.75. After fifty years you owe £11,739.09. After one hundred years you owe £1,378,061.23. (These figures are computed from the standard compound-interest formula: principal multiplied by one plus the rate, raised to the power of the number of years.) Compound interest produces explosive growth in the amount owed because the unpaid interest itself begins to earn interest. This mathematical structure makes the contemporary debt system structurally different from the earlier debt systems where interest was simple, capped, or periodically cancelled.

The combination of three features — money created as debt, debt charged at compound interest, and no routine debt cancellation — produces the system the four prior volumes of this series document. The structure requires that debt continue to grow. The growth of debt requires extraction from people who pay interest. The extraction concentrates wealth in the hands of those who receive the interest. Across four hundred years, the concentration becomes the system this series documents.

## IV. What a commercial bank actually is

A commercial bank, at its simplest, is a private business that takes in money from depositors, keeps that money safe, lends some of it out to borrowers, and creates new money in the process of lending, as Section II above explained. The bank earns its primary income from the difference between the interest it charges borrowers and the interest it pays depositors. This difference is called the **net interest margin**.

But banks do more than this. A commercial bank is also the operator of the country's **payments system** — the infrastructure that moves money between people and businesses. When you transfer money to someone else through a bank, you are not handing them physical cash. You are instructing your bank to reduce your account balance and increase theirs. If the recipient banks at the same bank as you, the transfer is an internal operation at that bank. If the recipient banks at a different bank, the two banks settle the transfer between themselves through their accounts at the central bank — that is, through the central bank reserves Section II introduced. The plumbing of money in the contemporary economy runs through the banks. The banks operate the rails on which money moves.

A commercial bank is also a **credit-rating institution**. When you apply for a loan, the bank assesses whether you are likely to repay. The bank's assessment determines whether you get the loan and at what rate of interest. The decisions banks make about who gets credit shape the economy. Industries with easy access to credit grow; industries without it do not. Individuals with easy access to credit can buy property and businesses; individuals without it cannot. The distribution of credit is, in a market economy, the distribution of opportunity. Volume One Chapter 9 of the series dossiers how the credit decisions of the largest commercial banks have shaped the contemporary distribution of wealth across the four-century period.

A commercial bank is, in the contemporary system, a profit-making private corporation. The shareholders own the bank. The shareholders extract **dividends** (cash payments to shareholders from the bank's profits). The shareholders elect the bank's board of directors. The board appoints the firm's senior management. The senior management makes the day-to-day decisions. The depositors — the people whose money is supposedly being safeguarded — have no governance role.

The major commercial banks operate within a framework set by central banks. The framework includes four principal sets of requirements. **Capital requirements** govern how much of the lender's lending must be backed by the shareholders' own equity in the bank, providing a buffer against losses. **Reserve requirements** govern how much central bank money the bank must hold. **Liquidity requirements** govern how much immediately available cash the bank must hold so it can meet depositors' withdrawal demands. **Interest-rate policy** is the central bank's setting of the rate at which it will lend to commercial banks; that rate then influences every other interest rate in the economy. The framework is what gives the central bank its leverage over the commercial banks. The framework is also what licenses the commercial banks to do what they do: create money at the moment of lending. The central bank sets the rules; the commercial banks operate within them; the system as a whole produces the contemporary money supply.

## V. What a central bank actually is, and where the architecture came from

A central bank, as Section II explained, is the institution that sits at the top of a country's banking system. Its principal functions, beyond what has already been named:

- **Issuing the national currency** — printing the notes, minting the coins, issuing the electronic reserves.
- **Setting monetary policy** — deciding the policy rate of interest, which influences every other rate in the economy. When the central bank raises the policy rate, borrowing across the economy becomes more expensive. When it lowers the policy rate, borrowing becomes cheaper.
- **Acting as the bank for the commercial banks** — holding the commercial banks' reserve accounts; settling inter-bank transfers; processing the wholesale payments that make up the largest share of money movement in any developed economy.
- **Acting as the bank for the government** — holding the government's accounts; processing the government's payments and receipts; managing the issuance of **government bonds** (the IOUs the government issues to raise money beyond what it collects in taxes).
- **Acting as lender of last resort** — when a commercial bank gets into trouble (depositors withdrawing more money than the bank can readily provide, in what is called a **bank run**), the central bank can lend the troubled bank emergency money to keep it solvent. This function was given to central banks in part to prevent the bank runs that had caused repeated financial crises in the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.
- **Regulating the commercial banks** — setting and supervising the capital, reserve, and liquidity requirements named in Section IV, either directly or through a separate regulatory body.

In every contemporary country with a developed financial system, the central bank is the key institution that connects the government, the commercial banks, and the broader economy. But "the government controls the central bank" is not quite right. The contemporary central banks are formally **independent** of elected governments. **Operational independence** is the principle that monetary policy decisions are made by central-bank officials without direct interference from politicians. This independence was granted by different paths in different countries. The US Congress granted it to the Federal Reserve over a long period across the twentieth century. The Bank of England received it in 1997 under the Labour government of Tony Blair. The European Central Bank held it from its founding by the 1992 Maastricht Treaty — the treaty that created the European Union and laid the groundwork for the euro. The official rationale is that monetary policy should be insulated from short-term political pressures. The operational consequence is that monetary policy is set by appointed central-bank officials whose accountability runs through narrow legal channels rather than through democratic political channels. Volume Two Chapter 11 of the series dossiers the political-class implications of central-bank independence.

The first modern central bank is generally considered to be the **Bank of Amsterdam** — in Dutch, the *Amsterdamsche Wisselbank*, literally the Amsterdam Exchange Bank. The Wisselbank was founded on 31 January 1609 by the city of Amsterdam. The scholarly literature is divided on whether the Wisselbank constitutes the first "true" central bank. Some scholars argue it does not. Their argument, set out in the Britannica entry on the institution among other places, is that because the Wisselbank did not manage a national currency or act as a lender of last resort, it does not meet the modern definition. Other scholars treat it as the first modern central bank. Their argument, set out in the Wikipedia consolidated entry on the institution and in Springer Nature's *Early Days of the Federal Reserve*, rests on three points. The Wisselbank was the first public bank to offer accounts not directly convertible to coin. It was the first institution to introduce a stable abstract unit of account separate from physical currency. And it was the operational model that every subsequent central bank inherited. The contestation is real. It is named here in the chapter text rather than parked in a footnote.

What is not contested is the Wisselbank's role and its influence. The Wisselbank was created to solve a practical problem: Amsterdam in the early seventeenth century was the principal trading hub of Europe. Ships from across the world docked there. The city was awash with hundreds of different kinds of coins — by some scholarly estimates as many as eight hundred coin types circulated in early-seventeenth-century Amsterdam — of varying gold and silver content. Merchants needed a reliable way to settle accounts in this multi-currency environment. The Wisselbank's solution was to accept deposits of any coin, assess each coin's true value by weight and metallic content, and credit the depositor's account in a stable unit of account called **bank money**. The bank money was reliable. It traded at a small but consistent premium over the chaotic mix of physical coins. Merchants deposited coins, received credits in bank money, settled their accounts using the credits, and could withdraw coins again if they needed them. The Wisselbank lasted 211 years, until 1820.

The Wisselbank had a key contemporary partner: the **Dutch East India Company** (in Dutch, the *Vereenigde Oostindische Compagnie*, known by its initials as the **VOC**). The VOC was the world's first formally listed joint-stock public corporation — that is, the first business whose ownership was divided into shares that could be traded among the public. The VOC was chartered on 20 March 1602 by the States General of the Netherlands, seven years before the Wisselbank was founded. The Amsterdam Bourse (the world's first stock exchange) opened to trade VOC shares. The Wisselbank's bank-money architecture solved the settlement problem that the high volume of VOC share-trading created. The VOC, the Bourse, and the Wisselbank together formed the operational core of the early-seventeenth-century Amsterdam financial complex — what historians of the period call the Dutch Financial Revolution. Volume One Chapter 1 of the series dossiers the Amsterdam complex of 1602-1609 as the institutional origin point of the contemporary financial architecture.

The Wisselbank was the model. The next iteration — and the more important one for the architecture this series documents — was the **Bank of England**.

The Bank of England was chartered on 27 July 1694 under royal charter from King William III, by an act of the English Parliament called the Bank of England Act 1694 (also known as the Tonnage Act 1694, after the duties on shipping that were earmarked to service the debt). The Bank's founding capital was raised by subscription from a syndicate of private merchants and was set at £1.2 million. The £1.2 million was lent to the government at an 8% annual interest rate, in exchange for the royal charter granting the subscribers exclusive banking privileges including the right to issue paper notes that the public could use as money. The Bank's first governor was Sir John Houblon. The Bank's founders included the Scottish merchant **William Paterson** (baptised 24 February 1658, died 22 January 1719), who proposed the scheme in 1691 in a pamphlet titled *A Brief Account of the Intended Bank of England*, and the merchant **Michael Godfrey**, who was killed at the Siege of Namur on 17 July 1695 while visiting the king's camp. Paterson, born in Tinwald, Dumfriesshire, Scotland, had spent time in Amsterdam in the 1680s and had studied the Wisselbank architecture there. The Bank of England Act passed Parliament with royal assent on 25 April 1694; commencement was 1 June 1694; the royal charter was granted 27 July 1694. The institutional record is in the Statutes of the Realm and the Bank of England's own founding archive.

The Bank of England was created to solve a problem the English Crown faced. King William III, who had taken the throne in the Glorious Revolution of 1688, was at war with France in what historians call the Nine Years' War (1688-1697). The wars of the period were expensive. The Crown's traditional credit had been damaged by the 1672 Stop of the Exchequer under King Charles II, when the Crown had suspended payments to its lenders and ruined the goldsmith-bankers who had been the principal source of Crown credit. By the early 1690s the Crown needed money and conventional sources were closed. Paterson's scheme offered something new: a permanent institutional creditor, capitalised by private subscribers, that would lend to the Crown not as a one-time loan but as a continuing institutional arrangement. The Crown would pay the Bank interest forever. The Bank would, in turn, issue notes against the Crown's debt that the public could use as money.

This arrangement created something that had not existed in this form before: **perpetual sovereign debt**. The government's £1.2 million loan from the Bank was not scheduled for repayment of principal. The Crown would pay the 8% interest annually, in perpetuity, while the principal would remain as a permanent institutional obligation. The Bank, in turn, issued notes against this debt that the public used as money. The notes were, in this sense, claims on the perpetual debt of the Crown. The money the public used was structurally fused with the government's obligation to the Bank. Money and debt — as Sections II and III have established — were no longer two separate things. They were two sides of the same institutional architecture.

There is a famous quotation widely attributed to Paterson that captures the architecture: **"The Bank hath benefit of interest on all moneys which it creates out of nothing."** This quotation is repeated across hundreds of secondary sources. It is also a quotation whose primary attestation cannot be verified. The phrase does not appear in Paterson's *A Brief Account of the Intended Bank of England* (1694), nor in the collected *Writings of William Paterson* (edited by Saxe Bannister, 1858), nor in any contemporary primary document the scholarly literature has found. The earliest documented appearance of the phrase is in Christopher Hollis, *The Two Nations: A Financial Study of English History* (Routledge, 1935). It was repeated by the historian Carroll Quigley in *Tragedy and Hope* (Macmillan, 1966), p. 49, without primary citation. The contemporary economist Meghnad Desai (Lord Desai, of the London School of Economics), in *The Route of All Evil: The Political Economy of Ezra Pound* (Faber & Faber, 2006), reports that his research could not turn up a primary reference for the attribution. The Wikiquote page for "Banking" and the Wikipedia Talk page for "William Paterson (banker)" both record the absence of any primary source. The quotation is, at present, a circulating attribution of unclear origin — possibly a Depression-era summary of Paterson's institutional argument rather than his exact words. The mechanism the quotation describes — that the institution's notes circulated as money and the Bank earned interest on what those notes represented — is well-documented in the 1694 founding architecture, independent of whether Paterson himself formulated it in those exact words. The chapter cites the quotation here in full awareness of its disputed status, because the mechanism it describes is real even if the formulation is not Paterson's. This is one of the contested-record items that the series names rather than papers over.

What is fully documented is the mechanism itself and what the mechanism produced. The English national debt grew from the 1694 founding loan of £1.2 million to roughly £50-54 million by 1720. The growth followed the War of the Spanish Succession (1701-1714) and the Duke of Marlborough's campaigns. (The precise 1720 figure varies slightly across scholarly sources. Duncan Needham's "A Very Short History of the National Debt" gives £54 million. *Economics Help*'s historical compilation gives £50 million.) By the end of the Napoleonic Wars in 1815 the British national debt had reached roughly **£1 billion** — more than 200% of British Gross Domestic Product at the time. The £1 billion 1815 peak is documented across three sources: Wikipedia's *History of the British national debt*, *Economics Help*'s historical analysis, and the Cambridge University Press *Historical Journal* article on national debt and political allegiance in eighteenth-century Britain. The 1815 figure represents a roughly 800-fold increase over the founding loan, across 121 years. The increase was produced primarily by the compounding of interest and the issuance of new debt to finance successive wars. The architecture works in the sense that it produces the outcomes built into its design.

Every contemporary national central bank operates on a variation of the Paterson architecture: money is created against government debt; the government owes the central bank or its bondholders interest in perpetuity; the public uses the money; the perpetual interest payments are extracted from the public through taxation. The Federal Reserve (founded 1913), the European Central Bank (founded 1998), the People's Bank of China (restructured 1995), the Bank of Japan (founded 1882) all operate variations on the same scheme. The architecture is what *The Last Mind* documents across four centuries of operation.

## VI. Putting the vocabulary together

You now have the foundational vocabulary the series uses.

**Money** is the social-and-legal agreement that a token represents a claim on goods, services, and labour. Every contemporary currency is fiat money, backed not by gold but by the willingness of users to accept it. The agreement is enforced by law and custom and can collapse when the collective willingness collapses.

**Debt** is a relationship in which one party owes something to another, recorded in a way both parties accept. Debt predates money in the documented historical record by roughly two thousand years. Modern debt typically carries interest. Compound interest produces explosive growth in unpaid debt. The contemporary system does not include the routine debt cancellation that earlier debt systems carried.

**A commercial bank** is a private business that takes in deposits, makes loans, creates new money at the moment of lending, processes payments, assesses creditworthiness, and extracts profit through the net interest margin. Banks are profit-making corporations owned by shareholders. Depositors have no governance role.

**A central bank** is the institution that sits at the top of the national banking system. It issues the national currency, sets monetary policy, acts as the bank for the commercial banks and for the government, acts as lender of last resort, and regulates the commercial banks. Most contemporary central banks are formally independent of elected governments. The first modern central bank was the Bank of Amsterdam (1609). The architecture of perpetual sovereign debt as the basis for money creation was operationalised by the Bank of England (1694) under William Paterson's scheme.

**The contemporary money system** rests on commercial banks creating money at the moment they make loans, against the framework set by central banks. Roughly ninety-seven percent of the money in circulation is bank deposit money created by commercial banks. Roughly three percent is central bank money — physical notes and coins, plus central bank reserves.

The system has been continuously in operation, in increasingly sophisticated form, from 1609 to the present — four hundred and seventeen years and counting at the time of this writing. The series documents how the system has operated across those four centuries, who has operated it, and what consequences the operation has produced. This chapter has given you the vocabulary on which that documentation rests.

## VII. What comes next

Chapter 0.2 of Part 0 introduces the foundational vocabulary of the second mask. It covers what a state actually is, what a political class is in the sense the series uses, and what regulatory capture means. It also covers what it means when a political system that presents itself as representing the people operates instead as the enforcement arm of a different institutional architecture. The vocabulary built in Chapter 0.2 supports Volume Two of the series.

Chapter 0.3 introduces the vocabulary of the third mask: institutions, authority, religious traditions versus religious institutions, and the distinction between the lived faith of communities and the captured hierarchies that claim to speak for them. The vocabulary supports Volume Three of the series.

Chapter 0.4 introduces the vocabulary of the fourth mask: what knowing is in the technical sense, how consensus forms, what peer review actually is, what fact-checking architecture actually does, and what contemporary artificial-intelligence systems actually are at the operational level. The vocabulary supports Volume Four of the series.

Chapter 0.5 closes Part 0 by drawing the four chapters of foundational vocabulary together and pointing forward to Chapter 1 — the formal statement of the account this volume documents.

The foundation is here. The rest of the building rests on it.

---



# Chapter 0.2: What a State Is, What a Political Class Is, What Capture Means

This is the second chapter of Part 0 of *Behind the Synthesis*. The previous chapter walked through money, debt, and banks — the foundational vocabulary of the first mask, the financial system. This chapter walks through the foundational vocabulary of the second mask: the state, sovereignty, the political class, and the concept of capture. By the end of this chapter the reader will have the vocabulary to follow every later chapter of this volume that touches on politics, and to read Volume Two of the series.

The chapter has the same standing approach as Chapter 0.1: every technical term is explained the first time it appears, in plain words, in the same sentence or the very next one. Where a term recurs many pages later, the meaning is anchored again in the surrounding prose. Every named person and every named institution carries enough introduction that a reader who has not encountered them before can follow.

## I. What a state actually is

There is a state above you. Wherever you are reading this, the territory you are sitting in is claimed by a state — a political organisation that says it has the right to make laws there, to collect taxes there, and to use force against you if you break those laws or refuse to pay those taxes. If you are in London, the state is the United Kingdom. If you are in Tokyo, the state is Japan. If you are in Lagos, the state is Nigeria. If you are in Buenos Aires, the state is Argentina. There are roughly two hundred such states in the contemporary world. Most of them have flags, currencies, armies, police forces, prisons, courts, and government buildings. Most of them recognise each other through an arrangement of formal diplomacy and a body called the United Nations (the international organisation founded on 24 October 1945 in the aftermath of the Second World War).

What is a state, at the foundational level?

The classical answer was given by the German sociologist **Max Weber** (1864-1920) in a lecture he delivered in Munich on 28 January 1919 — about two months after the end of the First World War — titled *Politik als Beruf*, which in English is *Politics as a Vocation*. Weber's definition, slightly paraphrased to match contemporary English, is this: a state is the human community that successfully claims the monopoly of the legitimate use of physical force within a given territory.

That sentence is doing a lot of work. The key words need to be unpacked one at a time.

**"Human community"** means the state is not a thing or a place but a set of people organised in a particular way. The buildings, the flags, the laws on paper — these are the visible signs. The state itself is the organisation of people who claim to operate the buildings, raise the flag, and enforce the laws.

**"Monopoly"** means *exclusive control* — one party, and only one, holds the relevant power. A monopoly in business means one company controls a market. A state's monopoly, in Weber's definition, is over a different thing.

**"Of the legitimate use of physical force"** is the thing the state has a monopoly of. This needs careful unpacking because Weber's claim is not that the state is the only one who ever uses force. Criminals use force. Private security companies use force. Domestic abusers use force. The state is not literally the only actor that ever resorts to physical coercion. What Weber claimed is that the state alone has the recognised *right* to use force, and the right to authorise others to use it. A private security firm can employ force, but only to the extent the state permits. A criminal gang can coerce people, but it is criminal precisely because it does so without state authorisation. The state, in Weber's framework, is the only source of the *right* to use force. Everyone else either has the state's permission, or operates against the state's claim.

**"Within a given territory"** is the geographic boundary. France's monopoly on the legitimate use of force ends at France's borders. Across the border in Germany, a different state holds the monopoly.

**"Successfully claims"** is the most subtle phrase. Weber put "successfully" in to mark that a state's monopoly is not just a legal ideal but an empirical fact that must be upheld in practice. A government that has lost control of its territory — like a contemporary state where armed militias control regions the central government cannot reach — has lost some or all of its monopoly. The government may still call itself a state. By Weber's standard, it has stopped being one in those regions.

This definition has been the standard reference in political sociology since 1919. The concept did not begin with Weber. He drew on earlier work, particularly two foundational texts in European political thought. The French jurist **Jean Bodin** (1530-1596) in his 1576 work *Les Six livres de la République* (*The Six Books of the Commonwealth*) set out the modern concept of **sovereignty** — the supreme and exclusive lawmaking authority of the state within its territory. The English philosopher **Thomas Hobbes** (1588-1679) in his 1651 book *Leviathan* described the state as an artificial sovereign that human beings create by surrendering their individual right to use force, in exchange for the security a single overwhelming authority can provide. Bodin gave the legal-philosophical foundation. Hobbes gave the political-philosophical foundation. Weber gave the sociological operationalisation. The three together form the standard classical answer to what a state is.

But the Weberian definition has come under contemporary pressure. The empirical record across the late twentieth and early twenty-first centuries has shown extensive privatisation of the state's coercive functions. Private military contractors — the most prominent of which has been the firm now known as **Constellis** (formerly Academi, formerly Xe Services, formerly Blackwater, founded by Erik Prince in 1997) — have carried out armed operations under contract to the United States and other governments across the Iraq, Afghanistan, and other contemporary conflicts. Volume Two Chapter 14 of *The Last Mind* dossiers the Constellis lineage and the broader private-military-contractor architecture. Across the same period, non-state armed groups have controlled territory in numerous regions — the Taliban in much of Afghanistan, Hezbollah in much of southern Lebanon, the various armed factions in Libya since 2011, the various armed factions in Yemen, and others. The Weberian monopoly has been eroded in practice even where states formally maintain it. The political scientist Andreas Anter (Professor at the University of Erfurt) observes in his contribution to *The Oxford Handbook of Max Weber* (Oxford Academic, 2019) that the Weberian state is now more the exception than the rule globally. The point matters because the series treats the contemporary state not as the sovereign actor of classical political theory but as one operational layer of a larger architecture — a mask, in the language of this volume.

So what is a state? At the formal level, it is the political organisation that claims the monopoly of legitimate force within a territory. At the operational level, in the contemporary period, it is the apparatus through which a particular pattern of social and economic arrangements is enforced. The chapters that follow will return to the question of *whose* arrangements those are.

## II. How states make decisions: the textbook answer and the empirical answer

If you ask the textbook how a contemporary democratic state makes its decisions, the answer is something like this. Citizens vote in elections. Elected representatives make laws and form governments. Governments implement laws through bureaucracies. Independent courts check whether the laws and their implementation match the constitution. Free media inform citizens about all of the above, so citizens can vote in the next election with the relevant information. The system is, in the textbook, self-correcting through periodic elections and through institutional checks and balances.

This is the textbook story. Like the textbook story about banks in the previous chapter, it is not entirely wrong. Some of its elements describe parts of what actually happens. But the textbook story does not match the empirical record of how policy outcomes actually get produced in contemporary democratic states, and the gap between the textbook and the record is the central subject of Volume Two of this series.

The principal contemporary empirical study of the gap is *Testing Theories of American Politics: Elites, Interest Groups, and Average Citizens* (2014) by **Martin Gilens** (then Professor of Politics at Princeton University, now at UCLA) and **Benjamin Page** (Professor of Decision Making at Northwestern University), published in the journal *Perspectives on Politics*. The two researchers assembled a database of 1,779 specific US policy outcomes across the period 1981-2002. Alongside the outcomes they collected survey data on the policy preferences of three groups. The first group was average citizens, defined as the household at the fiftieth percentile of US income — around $51,000 per year in 2012 dollars. The second group was economic elites, defined as the household at the ninetieth percentile — around $146,000 per year. The third group was organised interest groups, further divided into business-oriented groups and mass-based groups. Gilens and Page then statistically tested four theoretical accounts of how American politics actually operates: majoritarian electoral democracy (the textbook), economic-elite domination (the elite-theory account), majoritarian pluralism (an interest-group account favouring mass-based groups), and biased pluralism (an interest-group account favouring business-oriented groups). Their central finding, in their own published wording: "economic elites and organized groups representing business interests have substantial independent impacts on U.S. government policy, while mass-based interest groups and average citizens have little or no independent influence." The data supported the economic-elite-domination and biased-pluralism accounts; the data did not support the textbook account.

The Gilens-Page study is dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 4 of the series. The study has been contested in the scholarly literature — Omar S. Bashir, in a 2015 paper in the journal *Research & Politics*, argued that the Gilens-Page statistical test underestimates citizen influence and noted that in head-to-head matchups in the dataset where citizens and elites disagreed, citizens received their preferred outcome roughly forty-seven percent of the time. The contestation is real and is named here in the chapter; Volume Two Chapter 4 engages it in detail. What is not contested is that average-citizen preferences have much smaller independent effects on US policy outcomes than business-interest preferences do — the contestation is about how much smaller. The textbook story of policy responsiveness to citizen preferences does not fit the data even on the most citizen-favourable reading of the data.

A separate body of empirical work, principally that of **Thomas Ferguson** (Professor of Political Science Emeritus at the University of Massachusetts Boston, born 1949), has documented the role of major investors in determining the policy options available within contemporary US elections. Ferguson's principal book is *Golden Rule: The Investment Theory of Party Competition and the Logic of Money-Driven Political Systems* (University of Chicago Press, 1995). His **investment theory of party competition** holds that political parties in money-driven electoral systems function as coalitions of major investors who agree on certain policy positions and find candidates to carry those positions to the voters. The voters then choose among options that have already been filtered by the investors. The Ferguson framework is dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 5.

These are the contemporary empirical studies. They are not the only studies. They do not produce a complete account of how American politics works. But they establish that the textbook story is, at minimum, badly incomplete — and at maximum, a description of something that does not exist. The same gap between textbook story and empirical record has been documented in the United Kingdom, France, Germany, Japan, Brazil, India, South Africa, and across the other principal contemporary democracies. Volume Two carries the country-by-country documentation across its fifty dossiers.

The pattern is this. Contemporary states formally describe themselves as democracies in which citizens determine policy through elections. Empirically, the policy outcomes track the preferences of a much smaller group than the citizenry. This much smaller group is what this volume — borrowing from a long tradition in political sociology — calls the political class.

## III. What a political class actually is

The term "political class" is older than its contemporary usage. It traces back to the Italian political theorist **Gaetano Mosca** (1858-1941). His principal work was *Elementi di scienza politica* (in English, *The Ruling Class*; first published 1896, with a revised and expanded edition in 1923). Mosca set out the basic claim that in every society — democratic, autocratic, monarchic, republican, ancient, modern — a small organised minority rules over a much larger unorganised majority. Mosca's term in Italian was *classe politica*; English translations rendered it as "political class" or "ruling class." Mosca's contemporary **Vilfredo Pareto** (1848-1923) developed a related framework in his 1916 *Trattato di sociologia generale* (*The Mind and Society*). The central political dynamic in Pareto's framework was the **circulation of elites** — the historical pattern by which one ruling minority is replaced by another rather than displaced by genuine popular rule. The German sociologist **Robert Michels** (1876-1936), in his 1911 *Political Parties* (*Zur Soziologie des Parteiwesens in der modernen Demokratie*), added the **iron law of oligarchy** — the empirical observation that all organisations, including democratic parties, develop oligarchical leadership structures over time as a consequence of the technical requirements of organising at scale. Mosca, Pareto, and Michels together — the so-called Italian school of elite theory, sometimes called the elitist school — set out the classical case that organised minorities always rule.

The classical elite theorists wrote in a context in which their claim was controversial. Liberal democratic theory in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries was attempting to describe Western political systems as genuinely representative of the popular will. The elite theorists pushed back: representative institutions, they argued, do not displace minority rule; they only change which minority rules and through what procedures. The Italian school is not without political complications — Pareto's later work was sympathetic to Mussolini's Italian Fascist regime, and Michels himself joined the Italian Fascist party in 1924.
