The classical elite framework can be deployed to defend authoritarian rule on the grounds that elite rule is inevitable, so resistance to a particular set of rulers is futile. The series rejects that deployment. The framework as the series uses it is descriptive, not prescriptive: it describes what the empirical record shows about who actually governs, not what ought to govern.

The contemporary operational use of "political class" — the use this series adopts — was developed in mid-twentieth-century American sociology, most influentially by **C. Wright Mills** (1916-1962), Professor of Sociology at Columbia University, in his 1956 book *The Power Elite* (Oxford University Press). Mills's claim, drawn from a study of mid-1950s American institutions, was that the United States had developed an integrated power elite drawn from three sectors: the corporate, the political, and the military. The members of the elite circulated among the three sectors — a senior corporate executive becoming a Cabinet secretary becoming a defence-firm board member, and similar paths. The elite shared educational backgrounds (the same Ivy League universities), social institutions (the same clubs, the same charity boards), and class outlook. They were not a conspiracy. They did not need to be. They were a sociologically identifiable group whose members made the principal decisions about the direction of American society and whose decisions tracked the interests of the group rather than the interests of the broader population. Mills's framework is dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 3 of the series and operationalised against the contemporary 2026 record across the rest of Volume Two.

So what is the contemporary political class? It is the sociologically identifiable group of people who hold the principal political decision-making positions in contemporary states, who circulate among elected office, senior bureaucratic appointment, corporate boards, think-tank fellowships, lobbying positions, and major-media commentary roles, and whose policy preferences — when in tension with the preferences of the broader population — generally prevail. The political class is not a club with a membership list. It is a network of overlapping networks, distinguishable by the empirical record of who circulates among the relevant positions and whose preferences track the policy outcomes.

A few features of the contemporary political class matter for what follows in this volume:

**It is transnational.** Members of the contemporary political class in one country often have more in common — educationally, socially, financially, professionally — with members of the political class in other countries than with the non-elite citizens of their own country. A US senator and a German cabinet minister and a Japanese finance ministry official and a French presidential advisor are likely to have attended the same kinds of universities, to attend the same kinds of conferences, to read the same kinds of publications, and to hold the same kinds of policy views on the major issues. The annual meeting of the **World Economic Forum** at Davos, Switzerland — an institution founded in 1971 by the German engineer Klaus Schwab and dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 6 of the series — is the most visible site of this transnational political-class coordination. The Bilderberg Meetings (founded 1954), the Trilateral Commission (founded 1973), and the Atlantic Council (founded 1961) are others, all dossiered in Volume Two Chapter 6.

**It is integrated with the financial mask.** Members of the political class typically have major personal financial interests — investments, shareholdings, board positions, family wealth — in the financial sector and the major industries the political class regulates. The pattern is documented across every contemporary major democracy. Volume Two Chapters 1 and 7 dossier the US case at length.

**It is also captured by the consensus-knowledge mask.** Members of the political class typically share the consensus positions on issues from monetary policy to public health to climate response to AI safety — positions that have been shaped by the captured-knowledge apparatus documented in Volume Four. The political class does not need to consciously align with the financial mask because the consensus-knowledge mask has already aligned the political class's beliefs with the financial mask's interests. Volume Four Chapter 15 dossiers the documented mechanism by which this alignment is produced.

The political class is the second of the four masks the series documents. Volume Two dossiers it in operational detail. This chapter has introduced the vocabulary. The next section walks through the third concept the chapter needs to establish: capture.

## IV. What capture actually means

The textbook story about democratic government, as the previous section noted, has a layer beyond elections. Even granting that elections do not always produce policy outcomes that match citizen preferences, the textbook says there is a backup mechanism. Independent regulatory agencies — the bodies set up to police particular sectors of the economy on behalf of the public — are supposed to act in the public interest even when politicians do not. The Food and Drug Administration is supposed to ensure medicines are safe. The Securities and Exchange Commission is supposed to ensure financial markets are honest. The Environmental Protection Agency is supposed to ensure factories do not poison the air and water. The Federal Communications Commission is supposed to ensure broadcasters serve the public interest. And so on across the regulatory architecture of every contemporary state.

The textbook story about regulatory agencies is that they are staffed by technical experts insulated from political and industry pressure, who apply objective standards in the public interest. This is the **public-interest theory of regulation** — the position that regulatory agencies do what they are set up to do, namely protect the public from the harms that unregulated markets would otherwise produce.

Empirically, this story does not match the record. The mismatch was first systematically theorised by the American economist **George Stigler** (1911-1991), Professor at the University of Chicago from 1958 until his death and recipient of the Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences in 1982. Stigler's foundational paper is "The Theory of Economic Regulation," published in the *Bell Journal of Economics and Management Science* in Spring 1971. The paper drew on Stigler's earlier empirical work — in particular his 1962 study of US electricity-rate regulation with Claire Friedland in the *Journal of Law and Economics*, which had found that rate regulation had little independent effect on actual electricity rates. The empirical anomaly demanded explanation. The textbook said regulation protected consumers. The data said regulation often did not. Why?

Stigler's answer is what the literature now calls the **theory of regulatory capture**. The basic claim, in plain language, is this. Regulation is a service produced by the political system. Like any service, it goes to whoever can pay for it and can organise to demand it. The regulated industry has concentrated interests (large stakes per actor) and the resources to organise effective lobbying, campaign contributions, post-regulatory employment offers, and information control. The public has dispersed interests (small stakes per individual citizen) and limited capacity to monitor regulatory outcomes or organise effective demand for public-interest regulation. The predictable result, Stigler argued, is that regulatory agencies — over time, and absent extraordinary counter-pressure — end up serving the regulated industry rather than the public. The agency is "captured" by the industry it is meant to regulate. The regulatory function is converted from public protection to private benefit.

Stigler's 1971 paper made the capture concept respectable in mainstream economics. Subsequent work by Stigler's University of Chicago colleagues **Sam Peltzman** (in a 1976 *Journal of Law and Economics* paper) and **Gary S. Becker** (in a 1983 *Quarterly Journal of Economics* paper, both Chicago, Becker Nobel 1992) generalised the framework to model competition among different interest groups for regulatory outcomes, rather than treating capture as a one-way producer-takeover. The contemporary scholarly synthesis is the 2014 Cambridge University Press volume *Preventing Regulatory Capture: Special Interest Influence and How to Limit It*, edited by Daniel Carpenter (Harvard) and David A. Moss (Harvard Business School), which assembles fifty years of empirical work on capture across regulatory sectors.

What does capture look like in practice? Three contemporary cases that the series dossiers at length:

**The 2008 financial crisis.** The principal US regulatory agencies overseeing the financial system — the Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC), the Federal Reserve, the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency, and the Office of Thrift Supervision — had been, by the time of the crisis, captured by the financial industry they were meant to regulate. The SEC's 2004 decision to exempt the five largest investment banks from net-capital-rule limits is one of the canonical capture decisions of the period; the documented pattern of revolving-door movement of senior personnel between the SEC and Wall Street is another. Volume One Chapter 8 and Volume Two Chapter 7 dossier the financial-regulatory capture record.

**The 2010 Deepwater Horizon oil spill.** The US Minerals Management Service (MMS), the federal agency that regulated offshore oil drilling at the time, was found in the post-disaster investigations to have been captured by the offshore oil industry. The pattern included regulators accepting meals, travel, and gifts from industry; allowing industry to self-certify safety procedures the MMS was meant to verify; and routinely waiving safety requirements. The Obama administration broke up the MMS into three successor agencies in 2010 specifically in response to the documented capture. Volume Two Chapter 12 dossiers the case.

**The Boeing 737 MAX certification.** The US Federal Aviation Administration (FAA) is the federal agency responsible for certifying aircraft as safe to fly. By the time of the two 737 MAX crashes — Lion Air Flight 610 in 2018 and Ethiopian Airlines Flight 302 in 2019, which together killed 346 people — the FAA had delegated much of the safety-certification process for the 737 MAX back to Boeing itself. The post-crash investigations established that Boeing engineers had downplayed the risks of the flight-control system (MCAS) responsible for both crashes and that the FAA had not independently verified Boeing's safety claims. The certification process was, in the documented record, a captured one. Volume One Chapter 16 and Volume Two Chapter 13 dossier the case.

These are three among many. The pattern across them is consistent with Stigler's theoretical account: concentrated industry interests, dispersed public interests, captured regulatory outcomes, and identifiable disasters that result from the capture. The mechanism is general. The contemporary regulatory architecture of every major state shows the pattern to some degree, in some sectors. The series dossiers the major contemporary capture cases.

## V. The revolving door — how capture operates in practice

If you want to see capture operating, look at where regulatory personnel come from and where they go.

The technical term for the documented pattern of personnel exchange between regulatory agencies and the industries they regulate is the **revolving door**. The pattern: a person works for some years at a major firm in the regulated industry, then moves to a senior position at the regulatory agency that oversees the industry, then — after some years at the agency — moves back to the industry, often at a senior level and at sharply increased compensation. The pattern repeats across many sectors and across most major contemporary states.

The empirical scholarship on the revolving door is now extensive. The foundational US studies include J. E. Cohen, "The Dynamics of the 'Revolving Door' on the FCC" (*American Journal of Political Science*, November 1986), examining the Federal Communications Commission; and R. D. Eckert, "The Life Cycle of Regulatory Commissioners" (*Journal of Law and Economics*, April 1981), on US regulatory-commissioner career trajectories. The contemporary tracking organisations include **Project On Government Oversight** (POGO, founded 1981, headquartered in Washington DC), which maintains a publicly searchable revolving-door database for US federal regulatory positions, and **OpenSecrets** (the Center for Responsive Politics, founded 1983), which maintains a parallel database tracking former US government officials' movement to lobbying and corporate positions.

What the revolving-door data show, consistently, is that the senior personnel of the principal US regulatory agencies move predominantly between government service and the industries those agencies regulate. The same personnel architecture exists in the United Kingdom (where the relevant tracking is done by Transparency International UK and by the Civil Service Commission's reporting on appointments by former senior civil servants), in the European Union (where the tracking is done by Corporate Europe Observatory and by the European Ombudsman), and in most other contemporary major democracies.

The revolving door is the mechanism by which capture operates in practice. The regulators know they may someday work for the regulated industry. The regulated industry knows it can offer the regulators future employment. The career trajectories of senior regulatory personnel and senior industry personnel are intertwined. The independence of judgement the textbook describes does not survive intact under those conditions.

The revolving door is also one of the contemporary signatures of the political class as Section III described it. A senior regulator who moves to a senior industry position, then to a senior position in a presidential administration, then to a senior position on a corporate board, is a member of the political class in operation. The career trajectory is the political class living its working life.

## VI. The supranational layer

The classical state — the Weberian state of Section I — was a bounded territorial entity with monopoly authority within its borders. The contemporary state has been deeply modified by integration into supranational structures: institutions, agreements, and architectures that operate above the level of the individual state and to which the individual state has, in varying degrees, transferred some of its sovereign authority.

The principal contemporary supranational institutions, with their founding dates and contemporary functions:

**The United Nations** (UN), founded 24 October 1945 in the immediate aftermath of the Second World War. The principal contemporary forum for diplomatic relations among states. Headquarters in New York City.

**The International Monetary Fund** (IMF), founded at the Bretton Woods Conference (1-22 July 1944, in Bretton Woods, New Hampshire) and opened for business in 1946. The principal contemporary lender to states in balance-of-payments crisis. The IMF's lending is typically conditioned on the borrowing state agreeing to a set of policy reforms — typically including reductions in government spending, opening of the economy to foreign capital and trade, and liberalisation of labour markets. This conditional-lending framework is known as **structural adjustment**. Volume Two Chapter 8 dossiers the operational record of structural adjustment across the post-1980 period.

**The World Bank Group**, founded at the same Bretton Woods Conference. The principal contemporary lender for development projects in lower-income countries, also typically with policy conditions attached. Headquartered in Washington DC.

**The World Trade Organization** (WTO), founded 1 January 1995 as the successor to the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT, established 1947). The principal contemporary forum for international trade rules and dispute settlement. Headquartered in Geneva, Switzerland.

**The G7** (Group of Seven), the informal annual meeting of the heads of state or government of the seven principal advanced economies — the United States, Canada, the United Kingdom, France, Germany, Italy, and Japan, plus the European Union. Founded as the G6 in 1975, expanded to G7 in 1976.

**The G20** (Group of Twenty), founded at the finance-minister level in 1999 in response to the 1997-1998 Asian financial crisis, and elevated to head-of-state level in 2008 in response to the global financial crisis. Twenty members: nineteen sovereign countries, the European Union, and (from 2023) the African Union.

**The Bank for International Settlements** (BIS), founded 17 May 1930 at The Hague Conference. The central bank for central banks. Sixty-three member central banks. Headquartered in Basel, Switzerland. The BIS is dossiered in Volume One Chapter 6.

**The Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development** (OECD), founded 30 September 1961 in Paris, France. The principal contemporary policy-coordination body for advanced-economy governments, with thirty-eight member countries.

**The North Atlantic Treaty Organization** (NATO), founded 4 April 1949 in Washington DC. The principal contemporary military alliance of the Western states, with thirty-two member countries.

These supranational institutions do not have a Weberian monopoly on the legitimate use of force. They cannot raise armies in their own name. They cannot tax citizens directly. They depend, formally, on the cooperation of member states. But in operational terms they have acquired considerable authority over the policy choices of those member states. An IMF lending agreement determines the budget choices of the borrowing state. A WTO trade ruling determines the regulatory choices of member states. A NATO commitment determines the military deployment of member states. The classical Weberian sovereignty has been extensively modified — in some accounts, hollowed out — by the layer of supranational architecture above it. Volume Two Chapters 6 through 8 dossier the contemporary supranational layer in operational detail. The fifth mask of this volume — the unity mask, the mask that hides the integration of the other four — operates largely through the supranational architecture.

## VII. Putting the vocabulary together

You now have the foundational vocabulary the series uses for the second mask.

**A state** is the political organisation that claims the monopoly of legitimate physical force within a territory. Classically defined by Max Weber in 1919, drawing on earlier work by Bodin (1576) and Hobbes (1651). The contemporary state has been much modified by privatisation of coercive functions, by erosion through non-state armed groups, and by integration into a supranational architecture.

**Sovereignty** is the supreme and exclusive lawmaking authority of a state within its territory. The concept was developed by Bodin in 1576 and has been the standard reference in legal-political philosophy since. Contemporary sovereignty is partial: states have transferred portions of their authority to supranational institutions and to private actors.

**The political class** is the sociologically identifiable group of people who hold the principal political decision-making positions in contemporary states, who circulate among elected office, senior bureaucratic appointment, corporate boards, think-tank fellowships, lobbying positions, and major-media commentary roles. The concept traces to the classical Italian elite theorists Mosca (1896), Pareto (1916), and Michels (1911), and was operationalised against the mid-twentieth-century American record by C. Wright Mills in *The Power Elite* (1956). Empirically, the contemporary political class is transnational, integrated with the financial mask, and pre-aligned with the consensus-knowledge mask.

**Regulatory capture** is the documented pattern by which regulatory agencies, set up to police particular sectors of the economy in the public interest, come to serve the interests of the regulated industry instead. The concept was operationalised by George Stigler in 1971 (Nobel 1982) and has become mainstream in economics. Capture operates through concentrated industry interests, dispersed public interests, the revolving door, and information control. Major cases include the 2008 financial crisis, the 2010 Deepwater Horizon spill, and the Boeing 737 MAX certification disaster.

**The revolving door** is the documented pattern of personnel exchange between regulatory agencies and the industries they regulate. It is the mechanism through which capture operates in practice.

**The supranational architecture** is the layer of institutions and agreements above the level of individual states to which contemporary states have transferred portions of their authority. Principal institutions: the UN (1945), the IMF (1944/1946), the World Bank (1944/1946), the WTO (1995), the G7 (1975-1976), the G20 (1999/2008), the BIS (1930), the OECD (1961), and NATO (1949).

These together form the political mask. Volume Two of *The Last Mind* dossiers the political class across fifty principal countries and figures. The next chapter of this volume — Chapter 0.3 — introduces the vocabulary of the third mask: institutions, authority, and the distinction between religious traditions and religious institutions, which Volume Three of the series dossiers.

The foundation continues. The mask, in this chapter, has been named.

---



# Chapter 0.3: What an Institution Is, What Authority Is, How Religious Institutions Differ From Religious Traditions

This is the third chapter of Part 0 of *Behind the Synthesis*. The previous two chapters walked through the foundational vocabulary of the first two masks: money, debt, and banks for the financial mask in Chapter 0.1; states, the political class, and capture for the political mask in Chapter 0.2. This chapter walks through the foundational vocabulary of the third mask: institutions, authority, religious traditions versus religious institutions, and the distinction that Volume Three of the series rests on. By the end of this chapter the reader will have the vocabulary to follow every later chapter of this volume that touches on religion or institutional authority, and to read Volume Three of the series.

The chapter holds the same standing approach as the previous two chapters: every technical term is explained the first time it appears, in plain words, in the same sentence or the very next one. Every named person and every named institution carries enough introduction that a reader who has not encountered them before can follow.

## I. What an institution actually is

The word *institution* is used loosely in everyday speech. People call a bank an institution. They call a university an institution. They call marriage an institution. They call the Catholic Church an institution. They sometimes call a long-running television show an institution. The word covers a wide range of things in common usage, and the looseness can obscure what the word means in the precise sense the social sciences use it.

At the foundational level, an institution is a *stable, patterned, rule-governed way of doing things that persists across time and across the particular people involved*. The key features in that definition need to be unpacked.

**Stable** means the institution lasts. Individual people who participate in it come and go. The institution outlives them. A king dies; the monarchy continues. A judge retires; the court continues. A priest leaves the parish; the parish continues. The institution is not the same as the people who currently staff it.

**Patterned** means the institution operates through repeated, recognisable practices. A wedding is recognisable as a wedding because it follows a pattern. A trial is recognisable as a trial because it follows a pattern. The pattern is what makes the activity an instance of the institution rather than something else.

**Rule-governed** means the institution operates according to expectations — written or unwritten — that participants are expected to follow. The expectations can be formal (laws, constitutions, codes of canon law) or informal (the unwritten norms of how one is supposed to behave in a particular context). Either way, the rules shape what people do.

**Persisting across time and across particular people** is what distinguishes an institution from a mere habit or a one-off arrangement. An institution can be traced backward in time to its founding and forward in time to the people who will participate in it after the current generation has passed.

The American economist **Douglass North** (1920-2015), recipient of the Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences in 1993, gave the most widely cited contemporary working definition in his 1990 book *Institutions, Institutional Change and Economic Performance* (Cambridge University Press). North's definition: institutions are "humanly devised constraints that shape interaction." The institution itself is the rules; the people and bodies that operate within those rules — what North called organisations — are something else. The rules of football are an institution; the football club is an organisation. The rules of contract law are an institution; the law firm is an organisation. The rules and procedures of the Catholic Church are an institution; the Vatican as a working body is an organisation. The distinction matters for what follows: when this volume names institutional religion as a mask, it is naming the rules-and-procedures that have come to govern the religious lives of billions of people, not only the working bodies (the dioceses, the seminaries, the patriarchates) that staff those rules at any given moment.

The classical sociological treatment of institutions traces to the French sociologist **Émile Durkheim** (1858-1917), the founding figure of the academic discipline of sociology. In his 1895 book *Les règles de la méthode sociologique* (*The Rules of Sociological Method*), Durkheim defined sociology itself as "the science of institutions, their genesis and their functioning." For Durkheim, the institution was the principal object of sociological study. Institutions were what gave societies their durable shape — what made it possible to speak of "French society" or "German society" as continuing things rather than collections of individuals who happen to be alive at a given moment.

The twentieth-century sociologists Peter L. Berger (1929-2017) and Thomas Luckmann (1927-2016), in their 1966 book *The Social Construction of Reality* (Doubleday/Anchor), gave the standard account of how institutions emerge. The account has three steps. First, *habitualisation*: any pattern of human action repeated often enough becomes a habit. Second, *typification*: when different people develop the same habits and recognise each other's habits as instances of the same type of action, the habit becomes a typified pattern — a shared category of action. Third, *institutionalisation*: when typified patterns are passed on to new people who encounter them as already-existing facts about how things are done, the typified pattern has become an institution. The institution is, in this account, congealed habit. It is something humans created, but by the time any individual person encounters it, it appears as an objective feature of the social world they did not create and cannot easily change.

This last point matters. Institutions feel solid. They feel like facts about how the world is. The reason they feel that way is that they are old enough and widespread enough that no individual person could have created them and no individual person can easily change them. But they were created by humans, and they can in principle be changed by humans. The series's argument depends on this: the institutions that comprise the four masks were built; they can be unbuilt or rebuilt; they are not facts of nature.

Institutions are everywhere in modern life. The family is an institution. The school is an institution. The hospital is an institution. The court is an institution. The legislature is an institution. The bank is an institution. The corporation is an institution. The university is an institution. The army is an institution. Marriage is an institution. Property is an institution. Citizenship is an institution. Each of them is a stable, patterned, rule-governed way of doing things that persists across time. Each of them shapes how the people inside it can act. Each of them was created by humans, exists because humans continue to participate in it, and would dissolve if humans stopped participating.

The mask this chapter is the gateway to — the religious mask — is built of institutions in this sense.

## II. What authority actually is

The word *authority* is older than the social sciences and was used in many ways before sociologists tried to make the concept precise. The contemporary working sense the series uses comes principally from Max Weber, whose work on the state was covered in Chapter 0.2 and whose work on authority is the next foundational element this chapter needs.

Weber's question was: when one person commands another, why does the other obey? The answer cannot always be force — most authority is obeyed without force being applied, and most situations in which authority is exercised do not even involve the threat of immediate force. A judge speaks; the lawyers respond; no one is being held at gunpoint. A bishop instructs; the parish priest complies; no physical compulsion is involved. A bureaucrat in a tax office writes a letter demanding payment; the recipient pays; no police officer is at the door. Why does authority work?

Weber's answer, set out in Part I Chapter III of his posthumously published *Wirtschaft und Gesellschaft* (*Economy and Society*, 1922) under the heading "The Types of Legitimate Domination," is that authority works because the people obeying it accept it as *legitimate* — as rightful. The acceptance can rest on any of three foundations. Weber called these the three pure types of legitimate authority.

**Traditional authority** rests on long-established custom and on the sanctity of immemorial traditions. The authority is legitimate because it has always existed in the present form, or because it traces back to founding figures whose decisions are now part of inherited tradition. The classical examples are hereditary monarchies (the king rules because his ancestors ruled), patriarchal family structures (the father commands because in this tradition fathers always have), tribal eldership (the elders decide because tradition assigns the role to elders). Traditional authority predominates in societies where the past is the principal source of legitimacy. It is fragile when the tradition itself is questioned.

**Charismatic authority** rests on the exceptional personal qualities of a particular leader, recognised by followers as exceptional. The word comes from the Greek *charisma*, meaning a gift of grace; Weber used it in the religious-historical sense to refer to leaders who appear to their followers to be divinely or supernaturally endowed. The classical examples in Weber's writing are religious founders (Weber discussed Jesus, Muhammad, and Buddha as charismatic figures whose followers obeyed them because of who they were rather than because of inherited position or formal office), revolutionary leaders who break with existing tradition, and the founders of new movements. Charismatic authority is intensely personal. It does not survive the leader without a process Weber called the routinisation of charisma — the conversion of the leader's personal authority into either traditional authority (an inherited succession, like the various Caliphates after Muhammad) or legal-rational authority (a formal office, like the papacy after the apostolic founders).

**Legal-rational authority** rests on impersonal rules and procedures applied uniformly. The authority is legitimate because the rules say so and the procedures have been followed. The classical example is the modern bureaucratic state and the contemporary corporation. A tax inspector has authority not because she is personally remarkable, and not because her family always held the office, but because the law assigns the authority to her position and she has been appointed to the position through the proper procedure. The authority is in the office, not in the person. Weber argued that legal-rational authority is the principal form of authority in contemporary modern societies — that the historical movement of the past several centuries has been from traditional to legal-rational, with occasional charismatic disruptions.

The Weberian classification is the standard reference in contemporary political and sociological scholarship on authority. Volume Two Chapter 2 of the series operationalises it against the contemporary record.

The classification is also limited in ways the chapter needs to flag. In real institutions, the three types blend. A contemporary monarchy may combine traditional authority (the king is king by hereditary right) with legal-rational authority (the monarchy's powers are defined by a written constitution). A contemporary political leader may combine charismatic authority (followers see the leader as exceptional) with legal-rational authority (the leader holds an office defined by law). A contemporary religious institution may combine all three (the office holds traditional sanctity, the office-holder is presented as having charismatic gifts, and the office is also defined by formal canon law). The three types are *ideal types* in Weber's sense — analytical reference points against which real cases can be measured — rather than mutually exclusive categories.

The classification has also been criticised for under-attending to a fourth foundation of authority that has become central in the contemporary period: legitimacy through the production of consensus knowledge. Authority that rests on credentialed expertise — the doctor, the scientist, the economist, the technocrat — does not fit neatly into any of Weber's three types. It is not traditional (the credentials are recent, not immemorial). It is not charismatic (the authority is supposed to come from the office and the training, not from the personal exceptionality of the holder). It is not purely legal-rational (the authority claims to rest on truth-of-the-matter, not only on properly-followed procedure). The contemporary captured-knowledge apparatus that Volume Four of this series dossiers operates largely through this fourth authority type, and Chapter 0.4 of this volume will return to it. For the present chapter, the Weberian three-fold typology is the foundation; the modifications come later.

## III. Religious traditions, religious institutions, and the distinction the series rests on

This section introduces the central pedagogic move of the chapter — the distinction between religious traditions and religious institutions, and why the distinction matters for everything Volume Three of *The Last Mind* documents.

Consider a religious community. Pick any one. Consider a village in southern Italy where most families have been Catholic for many generations. The religious life of the community happens through prayer, devotional practices around saints and the Madonna, the weekly mass at the parish church, the annual feast days, and the rites around birth, marriage, and death. It also happens through the daily woven habits of being a believing person in a community of believing people. The same village exists, in different forms, in Pakistan as a Muslim community, in Rajasthan as a Hindu community, in Sri Lanka as a Buddhist community, in Brooklyn as an Orthodox Jewish community. The forms differ. The pattern — a tradition of belief and practice carried by the lived faith of a community — is the same kind of thing.

Now consider the formal hierarchies that claim to speak for those communities. The Catholic village in southern Italy is, formally, under the authority of the local bishop, who is appointed by the Vatican, which is structured around the Pope, the College of Cardinals, the Synod of Bishops, and the bureaucracy called the Roman Curia. The Muslim village in Pakistan is, formally, under the authority of state-licensed religious bodies and the Council of Islamic Ideology. The Hindu village in Rajasthan is under no single global hierarchy — Hinduism has no equivalent of the papacy — but is in practice influenced by the various dharma-acharyas, the Shankaracharyas, the Rashtriya Swayamsevak Sangh, and other contemporary institutional bodies that claim to speak for Hindu tradition. The Buddhist village in Sri Lanka is under the authority of the Sangha — the order of ordained monks — which has its own institutional bodies. The Orthodox Jewish community in Brooklyn is under the authority of the rabbinical court (the *beit din*) and a network of recognised religious authorities.

These two things — the lived faith of the village community, and the formal institutional hierarchies that claim to speak for the faith — are not the same. They are also not entirely separate. The contemporary academic study of religion has, across the past several decades, developed a sizeable body of scholarship under the heading of **lived religion** that examines what religion actually does in ordinary people's lives, as distinct from what religious institutions formally pronounce. The principal contemporary figure of this scholarship is the American religious historian **Robert A. Orsi** (born 1953), Grace Craddock Nagle Chair in Catholic Studies at Northwestern University. Orsi's foundational study, *The Madonna of 115th Street: Faith and Community in Italian Harlem, 1880-1950* (Yale University Press, 1985), is an ethnographic and historical account of the devotional life of Italian-immigrant Catholics in Harlem across a seventy-year period. What Orsi documents is a richly lived religious practice — centred on the Madonna of Mount Carmel, on the annual feast, on home altars, on personal devotional relationships with saints — that operated alongside the formal institutional Catholicism of the New York archdiocese without being identical to it. The villagers' lived Catholicism and the archdiocesan institutional Catholicism were both Catholic. They were not the same thing. The institutional church often viewed the villagers' practices with suspicion, attempted to reform them, and sometimes attempted to suppress them. The villagers, for their part, sustained their devotional practice across decades and several generations regardless of what the institutional church said about it.

The American sociologist **Meredith McGuire** (born 1949), Professor Emerita of Sociology and Anthropology at Trinity University in San Antonio, gave the sociology of religion its principal contemporary statement of the lived-religion approach in her 2008 book *Lived Religion: Faith and Practice in Everyday Life* (Oxford University Press). McGuire's argument: sociological work on religion has historically focused on what religious institutions say — what doctrines they teach, what positions they take, what bodies they form — at the expense of what religious people actually do, believe, and experience in their daily lives. The two are often different. The methodologically responsible sociology of religion, in McGuire's account, has to start with the lived practice and then ask what role the institutional bodies play in it.

The Boston University sociologist **Nancy Ammerman** synthesised the field in her 2016 review article "Lived Religion as an Emerging Field: An Assessment of Its Contours and Frontiers" in the *Annual Review of Sociology*. The field, by Ammerman's account, is now established. The methodological commitment — to look at religion as practised before looking at religion as institutionally pronounced — is now mainstream in the academic study of religion.

The series adopts this distinction at the foundational level. When this volume and Volume Three speak of religious traditions, they mean the lived faith and practice of communities — the prayer, the devotion, the rites of passage, the moral commitments, the personal relationships with the sacred. When the series speaks of religious institutions, it means the formal hierarchies, bureaucracies, doctrinal bodies, financial structures, and political-coordination apparatus that claim to speak for the traditions. The two are not the same. The tradition is older. The institution is what was built on top of the tradition, ostensibly to serve it, and what often comes to operate against it.

Why does the distinction matter? Because the series's argument about the religious mask depends on it. The series does not argue that religion as such is a mask. The series argues that the institutional apparatus of contemporary religion — across every major tradition — has been captured by the same financial mechanism that Chapter 0.1 introduced and that Volume One dossiers. The lived religious tradition of communities is not, in the series's account, the captured object. The lived tradition is what the institution has built itself on top of, and what the institution exploits to extract resources, attention, and consent. The Mexican family lighting a candle at home to the Virgin of Guadalupe, the Sufi devotee at a shrine in Pakistan, the Hindu woman performing morning puja, the Buddhist layperson making offerings to monks for the dead, the Jewish family observing the sabbath — these are the lived tradition. The Vatican Bank, the Saudi-funded Muslim World League, the politicised RSS-Sangh Parivar in contemporary India, the Russian Orthodox Church under Patriarch Kirill operating as an arm of the Russian state, the Wall-Street-integrated megachurches of American evangelical Christianity — these are the captured institution. The distinction is important enough that the series treats it as foundational to everything Volume Three argues.

## IV. What religion is, at the foundational level

The previous section took for granted the term "religion" and discussed what religious traditions and religious institutions are. This section steps back and treats the foundational question: what is religion?

The contemporary academic study of religion is itself divided on whether "religion" can be coherently defined at all. Some scholars argue that the concept of religion as a separable domain of human life — distinct from culture, politics, philosophy, ethics, and social custom — is a recent invention of post-Enlightenment European intellectual history that does not apply well to most human societies across most of history. The traditions the contemporary West labels "Hinduism" or "Shinto" or "indigenous religion" did not historically refer to themselves as religions in the sense the Western category implies. The category is contested.

The chapter will use a working definition while flagging the contestation. The definition comes from Émile Durkheim, the same founding sociologist whose work on institutions opened the chapter. In his 1912 book *Les formes élémentaires de la vie religieuse* (*The Elementary Forms of Religious Life*; the standard contemporary English translation is by Karen E. Fields, Free Press, 1995), Durkheim defined religion as follows: "a unified system of beliefs and practices relative to sacred things, that is to say, things set apart and forbidden — beliefs and practices which unite into one single moral community called a Church, all those who adhere to them."

Like the Weberian definition of the state in Chapter 0.2, this sentence is doing a lot of work. The key elements:

**"A unified system of beliefs and practices"** — religion, in Durkheim's account, is not just a private mental state. It is an organised pattern of both ideas (beliefs) and actions (practices) that hangs together as a system rather than as a random collection.

**"Relative to sacred things, that is to say, things set apart and forbidden"** — Durkheim's central conceptual move is the distinction between the *sacred* and the *profane*. The profane is the world of ordinary mundane activity: working, eating, sleeping, moving through daily life. The sacred is the world of things treated as set apart and inviolable: ritual objects, holy places, blessed persons, divine names, consecrated times. Durkheim argued that all known religious beliefs share one common feature — they classify things into these two opposed categories, and the boundary between them is treated as absolute. The two cannot coexist in the same place or the same time. Religion is the collective practice of marking off and maintaining the distance between them.

**"Which unite into one single moral community called a Church"** — Durkheim used "Church" as a generic sociological term, not as a specifically Christian one. By Church he meant any community of people who share the same set of beliefs and practices around the same sacred things. A Hindu temple congregation is a Church in Durkheim's sense. A Muslim *ummah* is a Church. A Jewish congregation is a Church. The point is that religion is inherently collective. A person can have private spiritual experiences. Religion, in Durkheim's framework, is what happens when those experiences are organised into a shared community of believers.

The Durkheimian definition is what scholars call *functionalist* — it focuses on what religion does in society (provide a shared system of meaning, mark off the sacred, unite people in moral community) rather than on whether the religious beliefs are true. The functionalist approach is the standard contemporary sociological starting point for the academic study of religion. It is also limited. It does not capture what religion feels like from the inside. It does not engage the truth claims of religious traditions. It treats religion as a social phenomenon to be analysed rather than as a domain of human experience that might be encountered on its own terms. Orsi's lived-religion scholarship, named in the previous section, is partly a response to the limits of the functionalist approach — an attempt to take ordinary religious experience seriously without reducing it to social function. Both framings are present in the contemporary academic study of religion. The series uses both: the Durkheimian framework for the institutional analysis of religion, the lived-religion framework for the analysis of what religion does in the lives of ordinary believers. The chapter holds both definitions alongside each other rather than collapsing them into one.

A reader who finds these debates too abstract can step back to the operational point. There is something called religion. Most human beings across most of history have participated in it. Contemporary academic scholarship is divided on exactly how to define it, but the working definitions all involve some form of organised belief and practice around things treated as sacred, carried by communities that share the beliefs and practices. The series does not need to settle the definitional debates. It needs only the working sense of what religion is to do the institutional analysis it carries out.

## V. The major contemporary religious traditions

This section gives the reader the foundational orientation to the major contemporary religious traditions the series dossiers. The numbers come from the Pew Research Center's 2020 estimates, which remain the standard reference for contemporary global religious demographics.

**Christianity** is the world's largest religious tradition by adherents, with roughly 2.4 billion globally as of 2020. It traces to the first-century teachings of Jesus of Nazareth and the work of his followers across the eastern Mediterranean world. The principal contemporary institutional bodies of Christianity include several major branches. The **Roman Catholic Church** has around 1.4 billion adherents; it is headquartered in Vatican City and led by the Pope, structured around the College of Cardinals, the Synod of Bishops, and the bureaucratic apparatus called the Roman Curia. The **Eastern Orthodox communion** has around 220 million adherents; it is structured as a family of self-governing churches called *autocephalous patriarchates*, including the Russian Orthodox Church under Patriarch Kirill, the Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople, the Greek Orthodox Church, and others. The **Anglican Communion** has around 85 million adherents with the Archbishop of Canterbury at its head, though without the central authority of the Pope. The various Reformed and Lutheran bodies collectively hold around 150 million adherents. The Pentecostal and evangelical bodies collectively hold several hundred million. A long tail of independent and non-denominational bodies sits alongside these. Volume Three Chapters 1 through 12 dossier the contemporary Christian institutional architecture. Volume Three Chapter 6 dossiers the Vatican Bank (formally the Institute for the Works of Religion, IOR) and the contemporary Catholic financial apparatus. Volume Three Chapter 11 dossiers the Russian Orthodox Church's integration with Russian state geopolitics. Volume Three Chapter 18 dossiers the Magnifica humanitas Vatican-Anthropic AI co-launch and its institutional implications.

**Islam** is the world's second-largest tradition, with roughly 1.9 billion adherents globally as of 2020. It traces to the seventh-century revelations of the Prophet Muhammad and the establishment of the Muslim community in Medina and Mecca. The principal contemporary institutional architectures of Islam fall into several major groupings. The **Saudi-funded Sunni institutional apparatus** operates principally through the Muslim World League (founded in Mecca in 1962) and the Organisation of Islamic Cooperation (founded in Jeddah in 1969); both bodies were greatly expanded with the post-1973 oil-revenue surge. The **Iranian Shia institutional apparatus** operates through the Vilayat-e Faqih constitutional structure established under Ayatollah Khomeini after the 1979 Iranian Revolution; the principal seminaries are in Qom and Najaf, and the Office of the Supreme Leader holds the senior position. The **Turkish state-managed Sunni institutional apparatus** operates through the *Diyanet İşleri Başkanlığı*, the Presidency of Religious Affairs, founded 1924 with the secular Turkish republic. Alongside these state-aligned bodies sit the various Sufi orders that carry the lived tradition outside and sometimes against the institutional bodies, plus various independent and reformist movements. Volume Three Chapters 13 through 18 dossier the contemporary Islamic institutional architecture. Volume Three Chapter 14 dossiers the Saudi state funding of the global Sunni institutional apparatus from 1973 onward.

**Hinduism** is the world's third-largest tradition, with roughly 1.2 billion adherents globally as of 2020. It is the most institutionally diverse of the major traditions, with no central hierarchical structure analogous to the papacy. The lived practice is carried through a dense network of devotional traditions, monastic orders, regional temple complexes, hereditary priestly families, and household rituals. The principal contemporary institutional bodies of Hinduism fall into several groupings. The various *dharma-acharyas* and *matha-pithas* are the institutional centres established by the eighth-century philosopher Adi Shankara and others, with the four principal seats at Shringeri, Dwarka, Puri, and Jyotirmath. The **Rashtriya Swayamsevak Sangh** (RSS, founded 1925 in Nagpur by Keshav Baliram Hedgewar) and the broader Sangh Parivar of affiliated organisations include the Vishva Hindu Parishad (VHP) and the Bharatiya Janata Party (BJP) currently governing India under Prime Minister Narendra Modi. Alongside these sit the various Vaishnavite and Shaivite monastic orders, and the various reformist movements including the Ramakrishna-Vivekananda tradition and the Arya Samaj. Volume Three Chapters 19 through 21 dossier the contemporary Hindu institutional architecture and its political integration in contemporary India. Volume Three Chapter 20 dossiers the RSS-Sangh Parivar architecture and its operational alignment with the contemporary Indian state.

**Buddhism** is the world's fourth-largest tradition by adherents, with roughly 510 million globally as of 2020. It traces to the fifth-century-BCE teachings of Siddhartha Gautama, the historical Buddha, in what is now Bihar and Uttar Pradesh in northern India. The principal contemporary institutional bodies of Buddhism are organised along the three major traditions. **Theravada Buddhism** is the school predominant in Sri Lanka, Myanmar, Thailand, Laos, and Cambodia; it is organised through the Sangha — the order of ordained monks — with national-level hierarchies in each country. **Mahayana Buddhism** is the school predominant in China, Japan, Korea, and Vietnam, with the Chinese Buddhist Association, the Japanese sects, the Korean Jogye Order, and others. **Vajrayana Buddhism** is the school principally associated with Tibetan Buddhism; its major institutional bodies are now largely in exile after the 1959 flight of the Dalai Lama from Tibet to India following the Chinese annexation. Volume Three Chapters 22 through 24 dossier the contemporary Buddhist institutional architecture.

**Judaism** is a tradition of roughly 14 million adherents globally as of 2020, with the largest contemporary populations in Israel and the United States. The principal contemporary institutional bodies include: the **Chief Rabbinate of Israel** (the official rabbinical authority in the State of Israel, with Ashkenazi and Sephardi Chief Rabbis); the **Orthodox** denominational bodies; the **Conservative** denominational bodies; the **Reform** denominational bodies; and the **Reconstructionist** denominational bodies. Volume Three Chapters 25 and 26 dossier the contemporary Jewish institutional architecture.

**The religious nones** — people who do not identify with any organised religious tradition — comprise roughly 1.2 billion globally as of 2020. This category includes both the actively non-religious (atheists, agnostics, and the deliberately secular) and the spiritually-inclined-but-non-affiliated, whose religious practice is privately held and outside institutional bodies. The category is heterogeneous and growing rapidly across the contemporary West. Volume Three Chapter 27 dossiers the contemporary religious-nones architecture and what the institutional capture of the major traditions has produced in terms of religious disaffiliation.

These six categories — Christianity, Islam, Hinduism, Buddhism, Judaism, and the religious nones — together account for roughly 99% of the contemporary global population. The remaining 1% includes the smaller traditions: indigenous religions, Sikhism, Bahá'í, Jainism, Zoroastrianism, Shinto, and others. The series's institutional analysis focuses on the six major categories because they account for almost all of the institutional architecture under study, but the absence of detailed discussion of the smaller traditions in the series should not be read as a judgement about their importance to their adherents.

## VI. Religious institutional capture — the analogue of regulatory capture in the religious sphere

Chapter 0.2 introduced the concept of regulatory capture — the documented pattern by which regulatory agencies, set up to police particular sectors of the economy in the public interest, come to serve the regulated industry instead. The concept transfers to the religious-institutional sphere with modifications.

A religious institution is, in its formal self-description, an apparatus that serves a religious tradition. The Vatican exists to serve the Catholic faith. The Muslim World League exists to serve the worldwide Sunni community. The Sangha exists to serve the Buddhist dharma. The Patriarchate of Moscow exists to serve the Russian Orthodox Christian tradition. These are the formal missions. The institutions present themselves as the trustworthy operators of the traditions on behalf of the believers.

The contemporary record, dossiered across Volume Three of the series, shows the same pattern of capture that Chapter 0.2 described in the regulatory sphere. The institution that was set up to serve the tradition has, over time, come to serve interests that are not the tradition's. The interests can be financial — the Vatican Bank scandals from the early 1980s onward, dossiered in Volume Three Chapter 7, are a documented case. The interests can be political — the Russian Orthodox Church under Patriarch Kirill operating as an institutional arm of the Russian state's geopolitical posture, dossiered in Volume Three Chapter 11, is another. The interests can be geopolitical — the Saudi state's use of religious institutions to project influence across the Sunni world, dossiered in Volume Three Chapter 14, is a third. The interests can be nationalist — the Hindu nationalist institutional bodies' integration with the contemporary Indian state's domestic political agenda, dossiered in Volume Three Chapter 20, is a fourth. The pattern, across traditions, is consistent: the institution's stated mission and the institution's operational mission have diverged.

The mechanism is similar to regulatory capture. The institution has concentrated power; the lived community has diffuse power. The institution has financial resources; the lived community generally does not. The institution has formal authority recognised by states; the lived community has no equivalent formal status. The institution can claim to speak for the tradition; the lived community has no comparable platform from which to dispute the claim. Over time, the institution's claims to authority become the working definition of what the tradition is, while the lived practice of the tradition becomes something the institution acknowledges or ignores at its discretion.

Religious institutional capture often runs deeper than regulatory capture in the political-economic sphere. In regulatory capture, the captured authority is a delegated state power — Congress or Parliament can in principle revoke the regulator's mandate and start over. In religious institutional capture, the captured authority is a claim to spiritual legitimacy — the institution has, over centuries, claimed and obtained recognition as the rightful operator of the tradition itself. Reformist movements that attempt to recover the lived tradition from the captured institution face a harder structural problem than political reformers who attempt to recover the regulator from the industry. The reformer of religion has to either reclaim the institution from within (the path of the various reformist movements across traditions) or step outside the institution and rebuild the lived practice in a form the institution does not control. Both paths are documented across the historical record. Both are difficult. Both are dossiered in Volume Three at the operational level.

For the purposes of this chapter the point is foundational: when the series names the religious mask, it is naming the captured institutional apparatus. It is not naming the lived faith of communities. The mask is the apparatus the lived faith has been built on top of, and that has come to operate against the very communities it claims to serve.

## VII. Putting the vocabulary together

You now have the foundational vocabulary the series uses for the third mask.

**An institution** is a stable, patterned, rule-governed way of doing things that persists across time and across the particular people involved. The definition draws on Durkheim's foundational treatment, Berger and Luckmann's three-step account of how institutions emerge, and Douglass North's working definition of institutions as humanly devised constraints that shape interaction. Institutions are everywhere in modern life. They feel solid because they are old and widespread, but they were built by humans and can in principle be changed.

**Authority** is the recognised right to command obedience. Max Weber's classical typology distinguishes three pure types: **traditional authority** (legitimacy by long-established custom), **charismatic authority** (legitimacy by the exceptional personal qualities of a leader), and **legal-rational authority** (legitimacy by impersonal rules and procedures). Real authority is typically a blend of the three types, with a fourth type — authority through the production of consensus knowledge — having become central in the contemporary period.

**A religious tradition** is the lived faith and practice of a community: the prayer, the devotional practices, the rites of passage, the moral commitments, the personal relationships with the sacred, carried across generations by ordinary believers.

**A religious institution** is the formal hierarchical apparatus that claims to speak for the tradition: the doctrinal bodies, the bureaucracies, the financial structures, the political-coordination apparatus, the credentialed religious authorities. The institution and the tradition are not the same. The institution was built on top of the tradition, ostensibly to serve it, and often comes to operate against it.

**The lived-religion scholarship** — Robert Orsi, Meredith McGuire, Nancy Ammerman, David Hall, and others — is the contemporary academic tradition that takes the practice of ordinary believers as the starting point for understanding religion, rather than the formal pronouncements of religious institutions. The series adopts the lived-versus-institutional distinction as foundational.

**The major contemporary religious traditions** — Christianity, Islam, Hinduism, Buddhism, Judaism, and the various smaller traditions — together with the growing population of religious nones, comprise the contemporary global religious landscape. Each major tradition has been built on a lived community of faith and has had institutional apparatus constructed on top of that community. The institutional apparatus is, in the series's account, the captured object.

**Religious institutional capture** is the documented pattern by which religious institutions, set up to serve a religious tradition, have come to operate against the tradition or in service of interests external to it. The mechanism is analogous to regulatory capture but often runs deeper, because the captured asset is a claim to spiritual authority that is harder to revoke than a delegated political authority.

These together form the religious mask. Volume Three of *The Last Mind* dossiers the religious mask across twenty-seven chapters. The next chapter of this volume — Chapter 0.4 — introduces the vocabulary of the fourth mask: knowing, consensus, peer review, fact-checking, and what contemporary artificial-intelligence systems actually are, which Volume Four of the series dossiers.

The foundation continues. The third mask has been named.
